⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه (السادس: مستقيمة ومن وضعية والمماسة على النقطة الأولى باقية بالقياس إلى الحركة الوضعية وأما بالقياس إلى الحركة المستقيمة التي وقعت على السطح فكلا والسائل إنما أورد السؤال باعتبار هذه الحركة وقال : إنه لو كانت المماسة على النقطة الأولى باقية بالقياس إلى هذه الحركة كانت الكرة ساكنة بالقياس إلى هذه الحركة والمفروض تحركها بهذه الحركة، وأما ثانياً : فلأنه لو قرر السؤال هكذا إن المماسة بالنقطة المعينة على الأخرى لا تحصل إلا بعد الحركة ففي حال الحركة لا بد أن تكون الكرة ساكنة، وإن كانت النقطة الأولى من الكرة على النقطة الأولى من السطح كانت الكرة ساكنة. وإن كانت بنقطة أخرى على نقطة أخرى متوسطين بين النقطتين الأوليين والأخريين لزم خلاف المفروض لم يتجه أن يقال : المماسة الأولى باقية إلى حصول المماسة الثانية فإنها وقد تقررت بتبدل النقطة الأولى من الكرة. فالحق ما استفيد من الشفاء أن المماسة حال على الحركة على الخط وليس فيها مماسة على النقطة الكرة فرض الإمام من أنه لو ماست الكرة السطح بالخط لوجب أن ينطبق من الكرة خط على ما خطه السطح فيكون ذلك الخط مستقيماً لأن المنطبق على المستقيم مستقيم فتكون الكرة متصلة فمدفوع بأن استقامة الخط في الكرة إنما يلزم لو كان انطباقه على خط السطح دفعياً وأما إذا كان تدريجياً على ما هو اللازم هاهنا فإنما يلزم وجود الخط المستدير في الكرة والآخر فيه . قوله : (الوجه السادس إلخ) يعني هذا الوجه السابع أخذ ما هو بالقوة بالفعل والجواب الفرق بينهما .
The sixth aspect: (the ball's motion is) rectilinear and rotational, and the contact on the first point remains with respect to rotational motion, but with respect to the rectilinear motion that occurred on the surface, no. The questioner only raised the question with respect to this motion and said: if the contact on the first point remained with respect to this motion, the sphere would be stationary with respect to this motion, but it is assumed to be moving with this motion. Secondly, if the question is posed thus: the contact on a specific point on another does not occur except after motion, so during motion the sphere must be stationary. If the first point of the sphere is on the first point of the surface, the sphere is stationary. If it is with another point on another point, mediating between the first and last points, then the assumption is violated; it would not be correct to say: the first contact remains until the second contact occurs, for it has been established with the replacement of the first point of the sphere. The truth, as derived from the Shifa', is that contact occurs in motion along the line, and there is no contact on a point of the sphere. The Imam supposed that if the sphere contacted the surface along a line, then a line from the sphere must coincide with a line drawn on the surface, and that line would be straight because that which coincides with a straight line is straight, so the sphere would be continuous. But this is rebutted because the straightness of the line on the sphere would only be necessary if its coincidence with the line of the surface were instantaneous; but if it is gradual, as is required here, then it only entails the existence of a curved line in the sphere and the other in it. His statement: 'The sixth aspect, etc.' means this seventh aspect takes what is potential as actual, and the answer is the difference between them.
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth proof for atomism, revolving around the motion of a sphere (ball) on a surface. The proof considers two motions: rectilinear (forward) and rotational (spinning). The objector claims that if contact on the first point persists relative to rectilinear motion, the ball would be stationary—contradicting its motion. Alternatively, if contact only occurs after motion, the ball would be stationary during motion. The commentator explains that the truth, derived from Avicenna's Shifa', is that contact occurs along the line during motion, not at a discrete point. The Imam (al-Razi?) had argued that if the sphere contacted the surface along a line, then a line from the sphere would coincide with a line on the surface, making the line straight and the sphere continuous. But the response is that this would require instantaneous coincidence; gradual coincidence only produces a curved line. The gloss at the end notes that the sixth proof actually corresponds to the seventh, as it treats potential as actual, and the answer lies in distinguishing between potential and actual.

Key Terms
كرة (sphere)
دحرجة (rolling)
مماسة (contact)
حركة مستقيمة (rectilinear motion)
حركة وضعية (rotational motion)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the rolling ball contacts the surface at a discrete point at every instant of its motion.
Correction: The correct view is that contact occurs along the line of motion, not at a point, as stated in Avicenna's Shifa'.

Checkpoint
According to the true position derived from the Shifa', where does contact occur during a ball's motion?
A) At a single point
B) Along the line
C) At two points simultaneously
D) Not at all

Key Takeaway
The rolling ball argument demonstrates that contact during motion is along a line, not at a point, which preserves the possibility of motion without contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine a ball rolling, you likely assume it touches the ground at a single point at each instant. This proof shows that assumption leads to contradiction. In your own thinking about existence, what other 'obvious' assumptions might be hiding contradictions that you have never examined? Will you take the time to question them, or will you remain comfortable with what seems obvious?
?
The objector in this proof insists on point-contact, which makes motion impossible. Have you ever held onto a belief so tightly that it made you reject clear evidence of change or growth in your own life? Are you willing to let go of a cherished assumption if it contradicts reality, even if it is uncomfortable?
?
Avicenna's solution redefines contact as along a line, not a point. This shift in perspective resolves the paradox. Is there a relationship, a habit, or a spiritual practice in your life that you have been viewing in a too-rigid or 'point-like' way that actually requires a more continuous, flexible approach? What would change if you redefined your understanding of that connection?

The Imam's Argument and the Gradual vs Instantaneous Distinction
Al-Jurjani refutes an additional argument by the Imam about contact along a line and explains the distinction between gradual and instantaneous coincidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the additional objection raised by the Imam (al-Razi) regarding contact along a line and its rebuttal based on gradual coincidence.

Core Idea
The Imam's objection fails because it assumes instantaneous coincidence, while actual motion involves gradual coincidence.

After the main resolution, al-Jurjani presents an argument by the Imam that if the sphere contacts the surface along a line, then a line on the sphere must coincide with a line on the surface, implying the sphere is straight and thus continuous. The rebuttal distinguishes between instantaneous and gradual coincidence, showing that only gradual coincidence occurs, preserving the sphere's curvature.

Guided Path
1
Read the Imam's supposition and the rebuttal
Read the Arabic and translation for this part. Notice the logical flow: the Imam's claim, then the rebuttal.
2
Understand the claim that contact along a line implies a straight line
The Imam argues that if the sphere contacts along a line, then a line on the sphere must coincide with a straight line on the surface, making the line straight and the sphere continuous.
This would be problematic for the atomist view.
3
Grasp the distinction between instantaneous and gradual coincidence
The rebuttal states that straightness would only follow if the coincidence were instantaneous (all points touching at once). In gradual coincidence, as in rolling, the line remains curved.
This is the key logical distinction.
4
See how this distinction refutes the Imam's argument
Since the ball's contact is gradual, the Imam's conclusion does not follow; the sphere's line stays curved.
5
Note the gloss about the sixth aspect actually being the seventh
The gloss notes that this proof corresponds to the seventh aspect, treating potential as actual, and the answer is to distinguish between them.
This is a meta-commentary on the enumeration.

The sixth aspect: (the ball's motion is) rectilinear and rotational, and the contact on the first point remains with respect to rotational motion, but with respect to the rectilinear motion that occurred on the surface, no. Why does the Imam's argument about contact along a line fail?
A) The sphere does not actually contact along a line

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Imam's argument, did you instinctively assume it was correct before considering the rebuttal? How often do you accept an argument at face value without examining its hidden assumptions about time or process? Examine your own heart: in matters of faith or daily judgments, do you rush to conclusions without distinguishing between what is instantaneous and what is gradual in your own understanding? Commit to pausing to ask, 'Is this assuming instant change when reality is gradual?'
?
The rebuttal hinges on the distinction between potential and actual. In your own life, have you ever condemned someone or something based on an 'instantaneous' reading of their actions, ignoring the gradual process behind them? Consider a relationship or a personal struggle. How might you apply the insight that the contact of truth with your heart is gradual, not instantaneous, and that even a curved line can remain curved while still making contact? Will you allow God's guidance to take time in your life, or do you demand instantaneous transformation? Resolve to be patient with the gradual work of faith.
?
The Imam's argument seems logical but fails because it conflates potential with actual. Which areas of your life are you treating as 'actual' fixed realities that are actually only 'potential' stages? Your spiritual state? Your character? Your understanding of a person? In what ways are you demanding instantaneous resolution from God or from yourself, when He has designed the process to be gradual? Surrender the need for instant perfection and embrace the gradual movement of the sphere of your soul towards the surface of His mercy.

The Seventh Proof: The Mustard Seed Argument
Al-Jurjani presents the seventh proof for atomism and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the mustard seed argument for atomism and its refutation by distinguishing potential from actual division.

Core Idea
Infinite potential division does not imply actual infinite parts.

Al-Jurjani presents the seventh proof for atomism, which argues that without atoms, a mustard seed could cover the earth in infinite layers—an absurdity. The refutation clarifies that infinite division means only potential parts, not actual. This lesson explores the argument and its resolution.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage of the seventh proof and its translation. Notice the structure: the argument, then the refutation.
2
Identify the proof's logic
The proof says: if no atom, a mustard seed can divide infinitely, its layers will cover the earth, which is absurd. Therefore, atoms exist. Understand this syllogism.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Study the refutation
The refutation explains that infinite division does not mean the parts actually exist; they are potential. So the layers never actualize, removing the absurdity.
Key distinction: potential vs. actual infinity.
4
Reflect on the author's comment
The author notes that while these proofs can be answered dialectically, they offer persuasion. Consider the role of dialectic in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه ( السابع : لولا الجزء ) وانتهاء تقسيم الجسم إليه لكان يمكن أن تقسم الخردلة إلى صفائح غير متناهية فتغمر تلك الصفائح ( وجه الأرض) وتستر وجوه السموات ) وتفضل عليها بما لا يتناهى وأنه ضروري البطلان ) . ورد هذا بما عرفت من معنى لا تناهي الانقسام وامتناع خروج جميع الأقسام إلى الفعل وجوداً بل فرضاً أيضاً، قال المصنف : ) وبعض ذلك الذي ذكرناه من حجج المتكلمين على إثبات الجزء وتركب الجسم منه ( وإن كان يمكن الجواب عنه جدلاً ففيه للمنصف إقناع ) وطمأنينة باطن فارجع أنت إلى إنصافك في الأجوبة التي مر ذكرها . [ المقصد الخامس : الجسم البسيط واحد قابل للقسمة ] ( حجة الحكماء على أن الجسم) البسيط ( واحد متصل) في نفسه (قابل للقسمة إلى غير النهاية لا أنه مركب ) . قوله : (أي وليس بمركب أشار إلى أن قوله : أنه مركب ليس معطوفاً على قوله : إنه واحد كما هو الظاهر فيختل المعنى بل هو معطوف على قوله : واحد . قوله : ( بالمعنى الذي عرفت أي آخر المقصد الثالث بمعني أن الجسم من شأنه أن يقبل الانقسام دائماً إلخ، وقد ذكرناه مراراً إلا أن مقادير أجزاء الخردلة فلا استحالة يعني أن اللازم الاستواء في عد الأجزاء بأن يكون أجزاء كل منهما غير متناهية ولا استحالة فيه والمحال استواء مقداريهما وهو غير لازم ولا عبرة بما يقال من أن الاستواء في الأجزاء يستلزم الاستواء في المقدار ضرورة أن تفاوت المقادير إنما هو بتفاوت الاجزاء بمعنى أن ما يكون مقداره أعظم يكون أجزاؤه أكثر فما لا يكون أجزاؤه أكثر لا يكون مقداره أعظم . قوله : (فارجع أنت إلى إنصافك في الأجوبة التي إلخ ) إشارة أنه يمكن الجواب من جميعها
The seventh aspect: if there were no part (atom) and division of the body into it had an end, then it would be possible to divide a mustard seed into infinite layers, and those layers would cover the face of the earth and conceal the faces of the heavens and exceed them infinitely, and that is necessarily false. This is refuted by what you have understood of the meaning of infinite division and the impossibility of all divisions coming into actual existence, not even in supposition. The author said: 'And some of what we have mentioned of the proofs of the mutakallimun for establishing the part and the composition of the body from it, although it can be answered dialectically, yet for the impartial person it contains persuasion and inner tranquility. So you, return to your impartiality in the answers that have been mentioned.' [The fifth section: The simple body is one and divisible.] (The proof of the philosophers that the simple body) is one continuous in itself, divisible into infinity, not that it is composed. His statement: 'i.e., and not composed' indicates that his statement 'that it is composed' is not coordinated with his statement 'that it is one' as is apparent, for that would corrupt the meaning; rather, it is coordinated with his statement 'one'. His statement: 'in the sense you have understood' i.e., at the end of the third section, meaning that the body is such that it can always accept division, etc., and we have mentioned it repeatedly—except that the magnitudes of the parts of a mustard seed are not like the magnitudes of the parts of the sky, so there is no impossibility. That is, the necessary consequence is equality in the number of parts, i.e., that the parts of each are infinite, and there is no impossibility in that; the impossible is the equality of their magnitudes, which is not necessary. No attention is paid to what is said that equality in parts entails equality in magnitude, necessarily, because difference in magnitudes is only due to difference in parts, meaning that what has a greater magnitude has more parts; so what does not have more parts does not have a greater magnitude. His statement: 'So you return to your impartiality in the answers which etc.' indicates that answers can be given to all of them.
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh proof for atomism, the famous 'mustard seed' argument. It argues that if bodies were infinitely divisible, a mustard seed could be divided into infinite layers that would cover the entire earth and heavens, which is absurd. Therefore, division must end at indivisible parts (atoms). This proof is then refuted by the distinction: infinite division does not mean all parts exist actually—they exist only potentially. The author quotes al-Iji's concession that while these proofs can be answered dialectically, they provide persuasion for the impartial. Then the text transitions to the fifth section (maqsad) on the nature of body. The philosophers' view is presented: the simple body is one continuous entity, divisible infinitely, not composed of atoms. The commentator explains a grammatical point: the phrase 'not that it is composed' is not a separate clause but coordinated with 'one'. He then discusses the meaning of 'in the sense you have understood', referring back to the definition that body is potentially divisible infinitely. He clarifies that the equality in number of parts (both infinite) does not entail equality in magnitude, because magnitudes differ by the size of parts, not their number. The concluding remark urges the reader to judge the answers impartially.

Key Terms
جزء لا يتجزأ
خردلة
انقسام
غير متناه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a thing is divisible infinitely, it can be split into actual infinite parts of equal size.
Correction: The refutation shows that infinite division yields only potential parts, not actual. Magnitudes are not equal because the size of parts can differ.

Checkpoint
The refutation of the mustard seed argument relies on which distinction?
A) Potential vs. actual division
B) Finite vs. infinite number
C) Existence vs. non-existence
D) Simple vs. composite body

Key Takeaway
The mustard seed argument is refuted by the distinction between potential and actual infinity, highlighting a key principle in kalam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The refutation shows that infinite potential does not imply actual infinite parts. In your own life, do you treat your potential for good deeds (e.g., helping others, seeking knowledge) as if it will never be actualized, and so you never take the first step? What 'mustard seed' of action have you left undivided because you assume infinite time, when in reality only the actual matters?
?
The author calls the reader to 'return to your impartiality' in evaluating the arguments. When you confront doubts about your faith, do you genuinely examine both sides with fairness, or do you cling to comfortable answers? Are you willing to let the truth unsettle you?
?
The proof offers persuasion to the impartial, even if it is not demonstrative. Is your faith built on such persuasive arguments that bring inner tranquility, or on uncritical acceptance? Will you seek that impartiality in your own heart today?

The Simple Body: One and Infinitely Divisible
Al-Jurjani presents the philosophers' definition of a simple body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' definition of a simple body as one continuous entity divisible infinitely, not composed of atoms.

Core Idea
For philosophers, body is a continuous unity, not a composite of atoms.

After discussing proofs for atomism, the text turns to the philosophers' view. They define a simple body as one continuous substance, infinitely divisible in potential. This lesson explains that definition and clarifies a grammatical nuance in the text.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' definition
Read the passage and translation. Note the key phrase: 'one continuous in itself, divisible into infinity, not that it is composed.'
2
Understand the grammatical point
The commentator explains that 'not composed' is not a separate claim but coordinated with 'one'—the body is one (i.e., not composed). This clarifies the meaning.
3
Reflect on the sense of division
The sense of 'divisible into infinity' is potential, as earlier established. The body can always be divided further, not that it is actually divided.
4
Consider the objection about magnitude
The passage addresses a potential objection: if two things have infinite parts, their magnitudes should be equal. It replies that number of parts does not determine magnitude, but size of parts does.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه ( السابع : لولا الجزء ) وانتهاء تقسيم الجسم إليه لكان يمكن أن تقسم الخردلة إلى صفائح غير متناهية فتغمر تلك الصفائح ( وجه الأرض) وتستر وجوه السموات ) وتفضل عليها بما لا يتناهى وأنه ضروري البطلان ) . ورد هذا بما عرفت من معنى لا تناهي الانقسام وامتناع خروج جميع الأقسام إلى الفعل وجوداً بل فرضاً أيضاً، قال المصنف : ) وبعض ذلك الذي ذكرناه من حجج المتكلمين على إثبات الجزء وتركب الجسم منه ( وإن كان يمكن الجواب عنه جدلاً ففيه للمنصف إقناع ) وطمأنينة باطن فارجع أنت إلى إنصافك في الأجوبة التي مر ذكرها . [ المقصد الخامس : الجسم البسيط واحد قابل للقسمة ] ( حجة الحكماء على أن الجسم) البسيط ( واحد متصل) في نفسه (قابل للقسمة إلى غير النهاية لا أنه مركب ) . قوله : (أي وليس بمركب أشار إلى أن قوله : أنه مركب ليس معطوفاً على قوله : إنه واحد كما هو الظاهر فيختل المعنى بل هو معطوف على قوله : واحد . قوله : ( بالمعنى الذي عرفت أي آخر المقصد الثالث بمعني أن الجسم من شأنه أن يقبل الانقسام دائماً إلخ، وقد ذكرناه مراراً إلا أن مقادير أجزاء الخردلة فلا استحالة يعني أن اللازم الاستواء في عد الأجزاء بأن يكون أجزاء كل منهما غير متناهية ولا استحالة فيه والمحال استواء مقداريهما وهو غير لازم ولا عبرة بما يقال من أن الاستواء في الأجزاء يستلزم الاستواء في المقدار ضرورة أن تفاوت المقادير إنما هو بتفاوت الاجزاء بمعنى أن ما يكون مقداره أعظم يكون أجزاؤه أكثر فما لا يكون أجزاؤه أكثر لا يكون مقداره أعظم . قوله : (فارجع أنت إلى إنصافك في الأجوبة التي إلخ ) إشارة أنه يمكن الجواب من جميعها
The seventh aspect: if there were no part (atom) and division of the body into it had an end, then it would be possible to divide a mustard seed into infinite layers, and those layers would cover the face of the earth and conceal the faces of the heavens and exceed them infinitely, and that is necessarily false. This is refuted by what you have understood of the meaning of infinite division and the impossibility of all divisions coming into actual existence, not even in supposition. The author said: 'And some of what we have mentioned of the proofs of the mutakallimun for establishing the part and the composition of the body from it, although it can be answered dialectically, yet for the impartial person it contains persuasion and inner tranquility. So you, return to your impartiality in the answers that have been mentioned.' [The fifth section: The simple body is one and divisible.] (The proof of the philosophers that the simple body) is one continuous in itself, divisible into infinity, not that it is composed. His statement: 'i.e., and not composed' indicates that his statement 'that it is composed' is not coordinated with his statement 'that it is one' as is apparent, for that would corrupt the meaning; rather, it is coordinated with his statement 'one'. His statement: 'in the sense you have understood' i.e., at the end of the third section, meaning that the body is such that it can always accept division, etc., and we have mentioned it repeatedly—except that the magnitudes of the parts of a mustard seed are not like the magnitudes of the parts of the sky, so there is no impossibility. That is, the necessary consequence is equality in the number of parts, i.e., that the parts of each are infinite, and there is no impossibility in that; the impossible is the equality of their magnitudes, which is not necessary. No attention is paid to what is said that equality in parts entails equality in magnitude, necessarily, because difference in magnitudes is only due to difference in parts, meaning that what has a greater magnitude has more parts; so what does not have more parts does not have a greater magnitude. His statement: 'So you return to your impartiality in the answers which etc.' indicates that answers can be given to all of them.
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh proof for atomism, the famous 'mustard seed' argument. It argues that if bodies were infinitely divisible, a mustard seed could be divided into infinite layers that would cover the entire earth and heavens, which is absurd. Therefore, division must end at indivisible parts (atoms). This proof is then refuted by the distinction: infinite division does not mean all parts exist actually—they exist only potentially. The author quotes al-Iji's concession that while these proofs can be answered dialectically, they provide persuasion for the impartial. Then the text transitions to the fifth section (maqsad) on the nature of body. The philosophers' view is presented: the simple body is one continuous entity, divisible infinitely, not composed of atoms. The commentator explains a grammatical point: the phrase 'not that it is composed' is not a separate clause but coordinated with 'one'. He then discusses the meaning of 'in the sense you have understood', referring back to the definition that body is potentially divisible infinitely. He clarifies that the equality in number of parts (both infinite) does not entail equality in magnitude, because magnitudes differ by the size of parts, not their number. The concluding remark urges the reader to judge the answers impartially.

Key Terms
جسم بسيط
متصل
غير متناه
انقسام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If two bodies have infinite parts, they must have equal magnitude.
Correction: Magnitude depends on the size of each part, not the number (which is infinite in both). So equality does not follow.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers, the simple body is:
A) Composed of atoms
B) One and continuous
C) Actually divided infinitely
D) A composite of parts

Key Takeaway
The simple body is defined as one continuous entity, and the objection about infinite parts and equal magnitude is refuted by distinguishing part number from part size.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The philosophers argue that the body is one continuous whole, not a collection of parts. In your own life, do you experience yourself as a unified servant of Allah, or as fragmented by competing desires? What step can you take today to restore your inner unity (tawḥīd) in actions?
?
The lesson distinguishes between infinite number of parts and the magnitude (quality) of each part. Reflect on your deeds: do you often compare the 'quantity' of your worship (e.g., number of prayers) to others, neglecting the 'quality' of your presence before Allah? How can you shift focus to the sincerity (ikhlas) of each action?

The Two Alignment Arguments for Infinite Divisibility
Al-Jurjani presents the first two alignment arguments for infinite divisibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two alignment arguments (muḥādhāt) used by philosophers to prove infinite divisibility, and the key distinction between essentially located and accidental entities.

Core Idea
Distinct sides force division—unless the sides are just accidents.

The philosophers argue that any essentially located thing must have distinct directions, which implies it is divisible. Al-Jurjani presents two specific arguments from alignment: one based on the inherent directions of an object (right/left), and another based on illuminated and dark sides when facing the sun. Let's examine these arguments carefully.

Guided Path
1
Understand the concept of alignment
Alignment refers to the orientation of a body's sides relative to itself or an external object. The philosophers use this to argue that distinct sides imply division.
2
First argument: essential directions
Every essentially located thing has distinct opposite directions (right/left, front/back) by necessity. These directions are different, so the object must be divisible. Therefore no indivisible part exists.
Note that accidental points/lines/surfaces are not essentially located, so they escape this argument.
3
Second argument: illumination
If you compose a surface from indivisible parts and face it to the sun, the illuminated side is different from the dark side. Since these sides are different, the parts must be divisible.
This argument uses an external reference (sun) to show differentiation.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why the philosophers focus on 'essentially located' things. What would happen if directions were merely accidental? The answer and refutation will be in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أربعة : النوع الأول : ما يتعلق بالمحاذاة وذلك وجهان : الأول : كل متحيز بالذات يمينه غير يساره ضرورة وكذا سائر جهاته المتقابلة متغايرة فظهر أن المتحيز بالذات يجب أن يكون منقسماً في جميع الجهات فاستحال وجود الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ، وكذا وجود الخط والسطح الجوهريين فضلاً عن تركب الجسم منها بخلاف النقطة والخط والسطح العرضيين فإنها ليست بمتحيزة بذواتها حتى يتصور لها جهات مقتضية لانقسامها. الوجه الثاني : إنا إذا ركبنا صفحة من أجزاء لا تتجزأ ثم قابلنا بها الشمس فإِن الوجه المضيء من تلك الصفحة أي الوجه الذي إلى الشمس غير الوجه المظلم أي الذي إلينا وهذا أيضاً ضروري فوجب أن تكون تلك الأجزاء منقسمة. وقد أجيب عن هذين الوجهين بأن اللازم منهما تعدد الأطراف ويجوز أن يكون لشيء واحد غير منقسم في ذاته أطراف هي أعراض حالة فيه، ودفع هذا الجواب بأن الطرفين المحاذيين لليمين واليسار مثلاً، إن كانا جوهرين فهما جزءان للذي فرض غير منقسم وإن كانا عرضين فإما أن يكونا حالين في محل واحد بحيث تكون الإشارة إلى أحدهما عين الإشارة إلى الآخر، فيلزم أن يكون ما حاذى منه يمينه عين ما حاذى منه يساره وهو بديهي البطلان، وإما أن يكونا حالين
Four [types]. The first type: what pertains to alignment (al-muḥādhāt). That is two ways: First: Every essentially located thing has its right not its left by necessity, and likewise all its opposite directions are different; so it appears that the essentially located thing must be divided in all directions, so the existence of the indivisible part is impossible, likewise the existence of the essential line and surface, let alone the composition of body from them, unlike the accidental point, line, and surface, for they are not essentially located so as to have directions necessitating their division. The second way: If we compose a surface from indivisible parts and then face it toward the sun, the illuminated side of that surface (i.e., the side facing the sun) is different from the dark side (i.e., the side facing us); this too is necessary, so those parts must be divided. And it was answered concerning these two ways that what follows from them is the multiplicity of extremities, and it is possible for one thing undivided in itself to have extremities that are accidents inhering in it. And this answer was refuted: The two extremities aligned with right and left, for example, if they are two substances, then they are two parts of that which was assumed undivided; and if they are two accidents, then either they inhere in one locus such that the pointing to one is the same as the pointing to the other, which would entail that what aligns with its right is the same as what aligns with its left—which is obviously false—or they inhere [in two loci]...
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' first type of proof for infinite divisibility, based on alignment (muḥādhāt), along with its rebuttal. The author classifies four types, but only the first is detailed here, containing two arguments (wajhān). The first argument: every essentially located (mutaḥayyiz bi-dhāt) thing has distinct directions (e.g., right and left are not the same), so it must be divisible—therefore the indivisible part cannot exist. The second argument: if a surface is composed of indivisible parts and placed facing the sun, one side is illuminated facing the sun and the other dark facing us; these are different, so the parts must be divisible. The answer to both is that what is required is merely a multiplicity of extremities (aṭrāf), and these extremities could be accidents inhering in a single undivided substance, not parts of it. Then this answer is refuted: if the two extremities are substances, they are parts; if accidents, they must either inhere in one locus (making the right and left directions identical—absurd) or in two loci (implying division of the substance). The text cuts off at this second branch, but the argument is clear: the alignment argument fails because directions can be conceived as accidental relations without requiring actual division.

Key Terms
محاذاة
متحيز بالذات
جهات متقابلة
جزء لا يتجزأ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All things with distinct sides are divisible.
Correction: Only essentially located things (mutaḥayyiz bi-dhāt) are divisible; accidental points, lines, surfaces are not essentially located and thus not subject to this argument.

Checkpoint
What is the key distinction made at the end of the first alignment argument?
A) Accidental points, lines, and surfaces are not essentially located.
B) Indivisible parts exist only in thought.
C) The sun causes illumination.
D) Right and left are the same in a point.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers argue that distinct directions force division, but only for essentially located entities; accidental geometric entities escape this conclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The argument shows that every essentially located thing must have distinct right and left, light and dark. Your heart is also a container of faith and doubt. Are you trying to keep both sides without allowing the Light of truth to divide what must be separated? What side are you truly facing right now, and what change must you make today?
?
If every essentially located thing is divisible and dependent on parts, then you are not self-sufficient. How does this humbling truth confront your pride? In what area of your life are you living as if you are indivisible—invulnerable or independent from Allah? What would it look like to admit your constant need for Him today?

Answering the Alignment Arguments: Extremities as Accidents
Al-Jurjani presents the answer and its refutation for the alignment arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' answer to the alignment arguments—that distinct sides can be accidental extremities—and the refutation that exposes its flaws.

Core Idea
Distinct sides need not be parts; they could be accidental extremities.

The philosophers' alignment arguments seem to require that distinct sides imply division. But the answer suggests that these sides could be mere accidents (aʿrāḍ) inhering in a single undivided substance. Al-Jurjani then presents the refutation that dismantles this answer. Let's see how.

Guided Path
1
Recall the alignment arguments
Review that the philosophers argued distinct sides force division. The answer attempts to block this by saying sides are accidents, not parts.
2
Understand the answer (jawāb)
The answer says: what follows from the arguments is only a multiplicity of extremities (aṭrāf), and it is possible for a single undivided thing to have extremities that are accidents inhering in it.
3
Examine the refutation (dafʿ)
The refutation considers two cases: if the extremities are substances, they are parts; if accidents, they must either inhere in one locus (making directions identical – absurd) or in two loci (implying division).
The text cuts off at the second branch, but the implication is clear: the answer fails.
4
Reflect on the coherence
Notice that the refutation forces a dilemma: either the extremities are parts, or they are accidents that cannot consistently explain direction difference without implying division.
This lesson completes the discussion of the alignment arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أربعة : النوع الأول : ما يتعلق بالمحاذاة وذلك وجهان : الأول : كل متحيز بالذات يمينه غير يساره ضرورة وكذا سائر جهاته المتقابلة متغايرة فظهر أن المتحيز بالذات يجب أن يكون منقسماً في جميع الجهات فاستحال وجود الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ، وكذا وجود الخط والسطح الجوهريين فضلاً عن تركب الجسم منها بخلاف النقطة والخط والسطح العرضيين فإنها ليست بمتحيزة بذواتها حتى يتصور لها جهات مقتضية لانقسامها. الوجه الثاني : إنا إذا ركبنا صفحة من أجزاء لا تتجزأ ثم قابلنا بها الشمس فإِن الوجه المضيء من تلك الصفحة أي الوجه الذي إلى الشمس غير الوجه المظلم أي الذي إلينا وهذا أيضاً ضروري فوجب أن تكون تلك الأجزاء منقسمة. وقد أجيب عن هذين الوجهين بأن اللازم منهما تعدد الأطراف ويجوز أن يكون لشيء واحد غير منقسم في ذاته أطراف هي أعراض حالة فيه، ودفع هذا الجواب بأن الطرفين المحاذيين لليمين واليسار مثلاً، إن كانا جوهرين فهما جزءان للذي فرض غير منقسم وإن كانا عرضين فإما أن يكونا حالين في محل واحد بحيث تكون الإشارة إلى أحدهما عين الإشارة إلى الآخر، فيلزم أن يكون ما حاذى منه يمينه عين ما حاذى منه يساره وهو بديهي البطلان، وإما أن يكونا حالين
Four [types]. The first type: what pertains to alignment (al-muḥādhāt). That is two ways: First: Every essentially located thing has its right not its left by necessity, and likewise all its opposite directions are different; so it appears that the essentially located thing must be divided in all directions, so the existence of the indivisible part is impossible, likewise the existence of the essential line and surface, let alone the composition of body from them, unlike the accidental point, line, and surface, for they are not essentially located so as to have directions necessitating their division. The second way: If we compose a surface from indivisible parts and then face it toward the sun, the illuminated side of that surface (i.e., the side facing the sun) is different from the dark side (i.e., the side facing us); this too is necessary, so those parts must be divided. And it was answered concerning these two ways that what follows from them is the multiplicity of extremities, and it is possible for one thing undivided in itself to have extremities that are accidents inhering in it. And this answer was refuted: The two extremities aligned with right and left, for example, if they are two substances, then they are two parts of that which was assumed undivided; and if they are two accidents, then either they inhere in one locus such that the pointing to one is the same as the pointing to the other, which would entail that what aligns with its right is the same as what aligns with its left—which is obviously false—or they inhere [in two loci]...
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' first type of proof for infinite divisibility, based on alignment (muḥādhāt), along with its rebuttal. The author classifies four types, but only the first is detailed here, containing two arguments (wajhān). The first argument: every essentially located (mutaḥayyiz bi-dhāt) thing has distinct directions (e.g., right and left are not the same), so it must be divisible—therefore the indivisible part cannot exist. The second argument: if a surface is composed of indivisible parts and placed facing the sun, one side is illuminated facing the sun and the other dark facing us; these are different, so the parts must be divisible. The answer to both is that what is required is merely a multiplicity of extremities (aṭrāf), and these extremities could be accidents inhering in a single undivided substance, not parts of it. Then this answer is refuted: if the two extremities are substances, they are parts; if accidents, they must either inhere in one locus (making the right and left directions identical—absurd) or in two loci (implying division of the substance). The text cuts off at this second branch, but the argument is clear: the alignment argument fails because directions can be conceived as accidental relations without requiring actual division.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Distinct sides can always be accidental without implying division.
Correction: If the sides are accidents, they either inhere in one locus (making them identical) or require two loci (implying division).

Checkpoint
What is the consequence if the two extremities (right and left) are accidents inhering in one locus?
A) The substance is divisible.
B) The right and left directions become identical, which is absurd.

Key Takeaway
The answer that distinct sides are mere accidents fails because it leads to either absurd identity or division of the substance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

What is one belief you hold that you have never subjected to such rigorous examination? Will you commit to testing it today?
?
The refutation in this passage reveals that inconsistency leads to absurdity. Is there an area in your life where you accept inconsistency—perhaps in your actions or thoughts—rather than thinking through the consequences? What would it cost you to be consistent?
?
Al-Jurjani's logical dissection forces us to care about truth even in foundational details. How often do you avoid deep thinking because it is uncomfortable? What is the first step you will take this week to pursue a deeper understanding of one aspect of your faith?

The Middle Part Must Be Divisible: First Argument Against Indivisible Parts
Al-Jurjani presents the first argument against indivisible parts via the middle part's necessary division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that if a body is composed of indivisible parts, the middle part must be divisible, and why the interpenetration objection fails.

Core Idea
The middle part of an atomic chain must be divisible because it touches both ends differently.

Al-Jurjani begins his systematic refutation of the atomist theory with an argument based on contact and arrangement. He shows that assuming indivisible parts leads to a contradiction when considering the middle part of a linear composite. This passage lays out the core objection and a rebuttal to the counterargument of interpenetration.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the flow of the argument. Notice how Al-Jurjani sets up the case: if a body is composed of indivisible parts, they must be arranged contiguously; the middle part then must be divisible.
Pay attention to the logical structure: assumption, arrangement, middle's role, conclusion, objection, refutation.
2
Identify the key steps of the argument
Break down the argument: assumption, necessity of arrangement, middle part's function, conclusion of division. Also identify the objection (interpenetration) and the refutation.
3
Understand the refutation of interpenetration
Al-Jurjani gives two lines: a) interpenetration is impossible by intuition, b) even if granted, it eliminates arrangement and volume increase. Also the contact-precedes-interpenetration argument.
The refutation is crucial for defending the argument.
4
Examine the limit of the argument
Note the final caveat: the argument works only if interpenetration happens after creation, not if parts are created overlapping. Consider why this distinction matters.

النوع الثاني : ما يتعلق بالمماسة وهو أيضاً وجهان : الأول : لو تركب الجسم من أجزاء لا تتجزأ فليست تلك الأجزاء أجزاء لا تتجزأ هذا خلف لكونه اجتماعا للنقيضين بيانه أنه إذا تركب الجسم منها فلا بد لها من أن تكون مجتمعة مترتبة متلاصقة وإلا لم يكن هناك تركب حقيقة وحينئذ فلا شك أن الواقع من تلك الأجزاء في وسط الترتيب يحجب الطرفين عن التماس فما به يماس الوسط أحد الطرفين غير ما به يماس الطرف الآخر إذ لو كانا متحدين لم يكن الوسط حاجباً للطرفين بل كانا متماسين، وإذا كان الأمر كذلك فينقسم الجزء الوسط مع كونه غير منقسم لا يقال : لا نسلم ذلك أي حجب الوسط للطرفين حتى يلزم انقسامه لجواز التداخل بين تلك الأجزاء لأنا نقول : بطلانه ضروري فإن بديهة العقل شاهدة بأن المتحيز بذاته يمتنع أن يداخل مثله بحيث يصير حجمهما معا كحجم واحد منهما وإن سلم جواز التداخل جدلاً فيكون حيزهما أي حيز المتداخلين واحداً ولا يزداد بانضمام أحدهما إلى الآخر مقدار، وكذا إذا انضم إليهما رابع وخامس وغيرهما من الأجزاء بالغاً ما بلغ فلا يكون ثمة ترتيب بين الأجزاء ولا وسط ولا طرف ولا يحصل من تأليفها حجم زائد على حجم كل واحد منها وذلك كله خلاف المفروض لأنا فرضنا تركب الجسم الذي هو حجم ممتد في الجهات الثلاث تلك الأجزاء فلا بد أن من يكون بينها ترتيب، وأن يكون هناك وسط وطرف ومع هذا الذي ذكرناه من لزوم خلاف المفروض على تقدير التداخل نقول : فالمداخلة بين جزئين إنما تكون بعد المماسة بينهما فلا شك أن الملاقي من أحد الجزأين عند المماسة غير الملاقي منه عند المداخلة التامة فيلزم الانقسام في كل واحد من الجزأين ولا يذهب عليك أن لزوم الانقسام من التداخل إنما يتم إذا كان التداخل حادثا بعد وجود الأجزاء وانضمام بعضها إلى بعض، أما إذا كانت الأجزاء متداخلة في ابتداء الخلقة بأن خلقت كذلك فلا
The second type: what pertains to contact, and it also has two aspects. The first: if the body is composed of indivisible parts, then those parts are not indivisible parts; this is a contradiction because it is a conjunction of two opposites. Its explanation: when the body is composed of them, they must be gathered, ordered, and contiguous; otherwise there would be no real composition. At that point, there is no doubt that the part of those parts that is in the middle of the arrangement hides the two ends from contact, so that by which the middle touches one end is other than that by which it touches the other end, for if they were identical, the middle would not be a barrier for the two ends; rather they would be in contact. And if it is thus, then the middle part is divided despite being indivisible. It should not be said: we do not grant that, i.e., the hiding of the two ends by the middle, so that its division follows, because of the possibility of interpenetration between those parts. For we say: its falsity is necessary, for the intuition of the intellect testifies that that which is spatially extended in itself is impossible to interpenetrate something like it such that their volume together becomes like the volume of one of them. And if interpenetration is granted by way of argument, then their space, i.e., the space of the interpenetrating parts, is one, and it does not increase by the joining of one to the other in quantity. Similarly, if a fourth, fifth, and others of the parts join them, no matter how many, then there is no arrangement among the parts, no middle, no end, and from their composition there results no volume exceeding the volume of each one of them. All of that is contrary to what was assumed, for we assumed that the body, which is a volume extended in three dimensions, is composed of those parts, so there must be arrangement among them, and there must be a middle and an end. Despite what we have mentioned of the necessity of contradicting the assumption on the hypothesis of interpenetration, we say: interpenetration between two parts only occurs after contact between them. So there is no doubt that the meeting part from one of the two parts at the time of contact is other than the meeting part from it at the time of complete interpenetration, so division is necessary in each one of the two parts. And it is not hidden from you that the necessity of division from interpenetration is only realized if the interpenetration occurs after the existence of the parts and the joining of some to others. As for if the parts are mutually overlapping from the beginning of creation, i.e., they were created thus, then not.
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage contains the first aspect (al-wajh al-awwal) of the second type of arguments against the theory that bodies are composed of indivisible parts (atoms). The argument focuses on the middle part of a linear arrangement. Al-Jurjani begins by stating the type: arguments pertaining to contact (mumāssa). The first face: if the body is composed of indivisible parts, then those parts are not indivisible, because that would lead to a contradiction. The explanation: when a body is composed of such parts, they must be arranged contiguously. The part in the middle of the arrangement necessarily hides the two ends from direct contact with each other. Therefore, the middle part touches one end with one part of itself and the other end with another part of itself; if it touched both with the same part, it would not be a barrier. Hence the middle part, though assumed indivisible, must actually be divisible. An objection is then raised: one might deny that the middle hides the ends, because the parts could interpenetrate (tadākhul). Al-Jurjani refutes this by invoking intuition: two spatially extended things cannot interpenetrate so that their combined volume equals that of one; this is impossible. Even if interpenetration is granted, the space of both becomes one, and adding more parts does not increase volume, so there would be no arrangement, no middle, no end—contradicting the assumption of a body composed of parts. Furthermore, interpenetration occurs only after contact, and at contact the meeting part of each is distinct from the meeting part after complete interpenetration, so each part would be divided. However, this argument only works if interpenetration happens after the parts are created separately; if they were created already interpenetrating, then the division does not follow (and thus the argument does not apply to that scenario).

Key Terms
جزء لا يتجزأ (juzʾ lā yatajazzaʾ)
تركب (tarkīb)
مماسة (mumāssa)
تداخل (tadākhul)
حيز (ḥayyiz)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Atomists may think that interpenetration (tadākhul) of parts allows the middle part to remain indivisible while still acting as a barrier.
Correction: Al-Jurjani shows that interpenetration is either impossible (intuition) or leads to absurd consequences (no arrangement, no volume increase), and even if it occurs, it implies division because the contact point changes.

Checkpoint
According to the first face, why must the middle part be divisible if the body is composed of indivisible parts?
A) Because the middle part is larger than the end parts.
B) Because the middle part touches both ends with different parts of itself, implying division.
C) Because the middle part is assumed to be indivisible, so it must be divided.
D) Because the ends are smaller than the middle.

Key Takeaway
The first face of the second type demonstrates that assuming indivisible parts leads to a contradiction in the middle part of a linear composite, and the interpenetration objection does not salvage the theory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The argument shows that a middle part that hides the ends must be divided. What 'middle part' in your own faith—perhaps a habit or excuse—is preventing your knowledge from touching your action? Will you divide it today by choosing obedience?
?
Al-Jurjani says the intellect's intuition testifies against interpenetration. When have you allowed falsehood to 'interpenetrate' your heart, mixing with truth until your worship lost its due volume? What specific step will you take to extract it?
?
If interpenetration is granted, there is no increase in volume. In your practice of Islam, are you adding rituals without spiritual growth? Where is your worship lacking increase, and what contradiction are you overlooking?

The Second Face: Landing on a Meeting Point Implies Divisibility
Al-Jurjani presents the second argument and its first proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Jurjani argues that an indivisible part landing on the meeting point of two parts must be divisible, and how motion forces it onto such a meeting point.

Core Idea
An indivisible part cannot land on a meeting point without being divided.

Al-Jurjani now presents a second direct argument against atomism. He sets up a conditional: if an indivisible part were to land on the meeting point of two other parts, then it would be divisible. To prove the antecedent, he offers three proofs, the first of which involves motion. This lesson unpacks the logic of the conditional and the first proof in detail.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Note that the text cuts off mid-sentence; we have the conditional and the first proof fully.
2
Understand the conditional argument
Identify the two parts: (1) if an indivisible part lands on a meeting point, then it must be divisible; (2) the antecedent is true. See how al-Jurjani proves the implication by showing the part would touch two parts with different portions of itself.
3
Analyse the first proof from motion
Focus on the first proof: when the indivisible part moves from one location to another, it must be on the meeting point during motion. Why can't it be entirely in the start or end location?
This is the key step that forces the part onto the meeting point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه ( الثاني : لو جاز أن يقع جزء لا يتجزأ على ملتقى اثنين من الأجزاء لم يكن ذلك الجزء جزءاً لا يتجزأ بل كان منقسماً . والملزوم حق فاللازم أيضاً حق واللزوم بين فإنه يكون الجزء الواقع على ملتقاهما مماسا لهما لا بالكلية أي لا يجوز أن يكون بكليته مماسا لشيء منهما، وإلا لم يكن واقعاً على الملتقي بل على أحدهما. فوجب أن يكون ببعضه مماساً لأحدهما وببعضه مماساً للآخر ولا معنى للانقسام إلا ذلك، وأما حقية الملزوم أعني وقوعه على ملتقى جزأين فلوجوه ثلاثة : الأول : لا شك أنه أي الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ على تقدير وجوده يتحرك من جزء مثله إلى جزء آخر كذلك فاتصافه بالحركة إما عند كونه بتمامه في الجزء الأول أو في الجزء الثاني أو عند كونه على الملتقي والأولان باطلان لأنه أي كونه في أحد الجزأين حاصل إما قبل الحركة وهو كونه في الجزء الأول أو بعد الفراغ منها وهو كونه في الجزء الثاني فلا يتصور اتصافه بالحركة حال كونه في أحدهما ، وفي الثالث أعني اتصافه بالحركة حال كونه على ملتقاهما المطلوب. الثاني : من هذه الوجوه نفرض خطاً مركباً من أجزاء شفع كستة مثلاً . ونفرض فوق أحد طرفيه جزءاً وتحت الطرف الآخر من الخط جزءاً آخر ثم نفرض أنهما تحركا أي تحرك كل منهما إلى صوب الآخر على التبادل حركة على السوية فلا بد أن يتحاذيا قبل أن
The second aspect: if it were permissible for an indivisible part to fall on the meeting point of two parts, then that part would not be an indivisible part, but rather would be divided. The antecedent is true, so the consequent is also true, and the implication is evident. For the part that falls on their meeting point would be in contact with them not completely, i.e., it is not permissible for it to be completely in contact with either of them; otherwise it would not be on the meeting point but on one of them. So it must be that with some of it it is in contact with one of them, and with some of it it is in contact with the other; and division means nothing other than that. As for the truth of the antecedent, namely its falling on the meeting point of two parts, it has three proofs. The first: there is no doubt that it—i.e., the indivisible part, on the hypothesis of its existence—moves from a part like it to another part like it. So its characterization with motion is either when it is entirely in the first part, or in the second part, or when it is on the meeting point. The first two are false, because its being in one of the two parts occurs either before motion (i.e., its being in the first part) or after finishing it (i.e., its being in the second part), so it is inconceivable for it to be characterized by motion while it is in one of them. And the third—i.e., its characterization with motion while it is on their meeting point—is what is sought. The second: of these proofs, we assume a line composed of even parts, like six. And we assume above one of its ends a part, and below the other end of the line another part. Then we assume that they move—i.e., each of them moves toward the other side alternately, with equal motion—then they must coincide before ...
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage contains the second aspect (al-wajh al-thānī) of the arguments against indivisible parts. Here al-Jurjani argues that if an indivisible part were to land on the meeting point (multaqā) of two other parts, then that part would be divisible. The reasoning: it would have to touch each of the two parts with a different part of itself, which is exactly what division means. He then proves the premise (that such a landing occurs) with three proofs (wujūh). The first proof involves motion: when an indivisible part moves from one location to another, it must be either entirely in the first, entirely in the second, or on the meeting point between them. Since motion cannot be attributed to it while it is entirely in either location, it must be on the meeting point during motion. The second proof is geometrical: assume a line composed of an even number of parts (e.g., six), and place a part above one end and another below the other end; if they move toward each other evenly, they must meet at some point, implying that the moving parts land on the meeting point. The text cuts off before completing this proof, but the argument is that this meeting forces division.

Key Terms
ملتقى (multaqā)
ملتقى جزأين (multaqā juzʾayn)
انقسام (inqisām)
حركة (ḥaraka)
خط مركب (khaṭṭ murakkab)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that an indivisible part could simply 'leap' over the meeting point without being on it.
Correction: Al-Jurjani assumes continuous motion through space; the part must pass through every intermediate point, including the meeting point.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof of the second face, why must the indivisible part be on the meeting point when moving from one location to another?
A) Because it cannot be in two places at once.
B) Because if it were entirely in either location, it would not be moving; only the meeting point allows motion.
C) Because the part is too small to be seen.
D) Because the motion is instantaneous.

Key Takeaway
The indivisible part's motion forces it to be on the meeting point, making it divisible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How often do you assume something is 'indivisible' in your own beliefs—something you think is too basic to question? Al-Jurjani shows that even the smallest part, when examined through motion, reveals its divisibility. What belief in your life today will you examine more closely by pushing it to its logical limits, and what are you afraid you might find?
?
The part cannot remain in one state forever; it must move. In your spiritual journey, are you stuck in a state of 'no motion'—comfortable in one position or another—refusing to cross the meeting point where growth happens? What one step will you take today to stand on that meeting point and allow yourself to be transformed, even if it feels like being 'divided'?
?
Al-Jurjani uses pure logic to uncover a truth about the physical world. When it comes to your own life, do you avoid logical scrutiny of your actions and intentions because you fear they'll fall apart? Commit to one area today where you will apply the same rigorous reasoning: ask yourself, 'If I truly believe this, what must necessarily follow? And if it leads to a contradiction, am I willing to change?'

The First Proof: Motion of the Indivisible Part
Al-Iji presents the first proof that an indivisible part cannot land on a juncture.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that an indivisible part moving between two adjacent parts must occupy their juncture, thereby requiring division.

Core Idea
Motion cannot happen in a single location; it requires a boundary.

Theologians argued that if bodies are composed of indivisible parts, then motion between them forces a contradiction. Al-Iji presents the first proof: a moving part cannot be entirely in either endpoint, forcing it to straddle the juncture, which implies divisibility. This lesson walks through that argument and the objections raised against it.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument and identify the three possible states
Read the Arabic passage carefully. The argument posits three possible states for the moving part: entirely in the first part, entirely in the second part, or at the juncture between them.
Notice the logical structure: a trilemma.
2
Understand why the first two states are false
The passage argues that if the part is entirely in the first part, it is before motion begins; if entirely in the second, motion is complete. In neither case can it be described as moving.
Motion is a transition, not a static state.
3
See why the third state leads to the conclusion
The only remaining possibility is that the part is at the juncture while moving. But being at the juncture means it touches both parts simultaneously, which would require it to have parts itself. Thus the indivisible part cannot move without implying division.
The marginal comments address objections to the proof's assumptions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول : لا شك أنه ) أي الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ على تقدير وجوده ) يتحرك من جزء ( مثله ) إلى ( جزء (آخر) كذلك ( فاتصافه بالحركة إما عند كونه بتمامه في الجزء الأول (أو ) في الجزء ( الثاني : أو ) عند كونه ( على الملتقي والأولان ،باطلان لأنه أي كونه في أحد الجزأين حاصل (إما قبل الحركة وهو كونه في الجزء الأول ) أو بعد الفراغ منها ) وهو كونه في الجزء الثاني فلا يتصور اتصافه بالحركة حال كونه في أحدهما ، ( وفي الثالث ( أعني اتصافه بالحركة حال كونه على ملتقاهما ( المطلوب.
قوله : ( إنه يتحرك إلخ) هذا الوجه إنما يتم إذا وجد الجزء على الانفراد وأمكن حركته والقائلون بتركب الجسم من الأجزاء يمنعون وجود الحيز منفرداً فضلاً عن حركته.
قوله : ( وبعد الفراغ إلخ) أصحاب الجزء يقولون : الحركة الثاني فلا يسلمون كونه في الحيز الثاني بعد الفراغ منها . هي الكون الثاني في المكان
قوله : (أي كونه في أحد الجزأين حاصل اما قبل الحركة قيل: لم لا يجوز أن يكون هو على الجزء الأول حال الحركة بأن يكون مماسة عليه باقية زماناً ما في حال حركته في الجزء إذ هو حال الحركة لو لم يكن زائلاً بتماسه عن الجزء الأول يلزم انقسامه أو عدم حركته هذا خلف .
The first: There is no doubt that it (i.e., the indivisible part, assuming its existence) moves from one part (like it) to another part (similarly). So its being described with motion is either when it is entirely in the first part, or in the second part, or when it is at the juncture. The first two are false: because its being in one of the two parts occurs either before the motion (that is its being in the first part) or after completion of it (that is its being in the second part); thus it is not conceivable that it be described with motion while being in one of them. And in the third (i.e., its being described with motion while it is at their juncture) is what is sought.  He said: (It moves, etc.) This aspect is only valid if the part exists in isolation and its motion is possible, but those who assert composition of the body from parts deny the existence of an isolated space, let alone its motion.  He said: (And after completion, etc.) The proponents of the part say: motion is the second in-place, so they do not accept that it is in the second space after completion of it. It is the second in-place in the place.  He said: (i.e., its being in one of the two parts occurs either before motion) It is said: Why may it not be that it is on the first part during motion, such that its contiguity with it remains for a time while it is moving in the part? For if during motion it were not ceasing by its contact with the first part, then either its division or its non-motion would be necessary; this is a contradiction.
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage presents the first of three proofs that an indivisible part cannot land on the juncture of two other parts without itself being divided. The argument proceeds from the assumption that the indivisible part moves from one part to another. During such motion, the part must either be entirely in the first part, entirely in the second, or at the juncture. It cannot be described as moving while entirely in either part, because motion is a transition; if it is wholly in the first, it is not yet moving, and if wholly in the second, motion is complete. Therefore, the only moment at which motion can be attributed is when it is at the juncture, touching both parts. But touching two parts with its entirety is impossible without division; thus it must have parts. The marginal comments address objections: one notes that the argument assumes an isolated part, which atomists deny; another clarifies that atomists define motion as a second ‘in-place’ and reject the possibility of occupying the second space after motion; a third responds to a counterargument that the part might maintain contiguity with the first part during motion, which would imply either division or impossibility of motion.

Key Terms
جزء لا يتجزأ
ملتقى
حركة
مماسة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think motion can occur while a thing is wholly in one location.
Correction: The argument shows that if the part is entirely in one location, it is not moving; motion requires a boundary state.

Checkpoint
Why does the first proof argue that an indivisible part moving from one part to another must be at the juncture?
A) Because it cannot be in two places at once.
B) Because if it were entirely in either part, motion would not be occurring.
C) Because it must touch both parts simultaneously.
D) Because motion is defined as being at the juncture.

Key Takeaway
The first proof shows that an indivisible part moving between two parts must occupy the juncture, thereby entailing division.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This argument challenges the very foundation of how we think about matter. When you contemplate the intricacy of creation—the smallness of an atom, the precision of a cell—are you humbled by the limits of your own understanding, or do you take these wonders for granted? What specific step will you take today to deepen your awe of Allah's design?
?
The proof demands we abandon our assumptions and follow logic, even to uncomfortable conclusions. In your spiritual life, have you ever clung to a comfortable belief or practice without really examining it? What would it cost you to honestly critique your own faith habits?
?
If the smallest particle of creation cannot exist without a Sustainer upholding it at every moment, how does that change the way you view your own existence? Will you today, for five minutes, sit quietly and reflect on the fact that you are being re-created from moment to moment by Allah? Write down one practical change this reflection demands.

The Second Proof: A Line of Even Parts
Al-Iji uses a line of even parts to show an indivisible part at the midpoint must be divisible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Iji's thought experiment with a line of even parts demonstrates that the alleged indivisible part at the midpoint must be divisible.

Core Idea
A line of even parts forces the midpoint to be a juncture, making any part there divisible.

This lesson presents al-Iji's second proof against atomism. By constructing a line of even parts and moving two external parts towards each other, we show that the midpoint must be a juncture of two parts, implying division. This logical argument uses a thought experiment to challenge the existence of indivisible parts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the translation. Notice the set-up: a line of even parts, two moving parts, and the moment of alignment.
The marginal comments are not part of the main argument but offer objection and grammatical notes.
2
Visualize the line and motion
Imagine a line composed of six equal, indivisible parts. Place one part above the top end and one below the bottom end. Then move them towards each other at equal speeds.
The parts are 'indivisible' by assumption; we are testing that assumption.
3
Identify the alignment point
The two moving parts will align exactly at the midpoint of the line before they pass each other. That midpoint is the juncture of the third and fourth parts from each end.
If the line had six parts, the midpoint lies between part 3 and part 4.
4
Draw the conclusion
If an indivisible part were placed at that midpoint, it would have to touch both part 3 and part 4. This means it would be divided, contradicting its indivisibility. Therefore, no truly indivisible part exists.
The proof is a reductio ad absurdum of the atomist position.

Source Text

الثاني : ( من هذه الوجوه (نفرض خطاً) مركباً ( من أجزاء شفع كستة مثلاً . ( ونفرض فوق أحد طرفيه جزءاً وتحت) الطرف (الآخر) من الخط ( جزءاً ) آخر ( ثم ) نفرض أنهما ( تحركا ) أي تحرك كل منهما إلى صوب الآخر على التبادل حركة ) على السوية فلا بد أن يتحاذيا قبل أن يتجاوزا وذلك ) التحاذي إنما يكون ) على المنتصف ) من الخط ( إذا ) قد (فرضنا الحركتين سواء في السرعة والبطء، (وهو) أي منتصف الخط ( ملتقى الثالث والرابع) من تلك الأجزاء بالقياس إلى كل واحد من طرفي الخط كما يلوح بأدنى تأمل صادق .

The second: (Among these aspects) We suppose a line composed of even parts, e.g., six. And we suppose above one of its ends a part, and below the other end of the line another part. Then we suppose that they (the two parts) move, i.e., each moves towards the other alternately, with equal motion. Then inevitably they will be aligned before they pass each other, and that alignment occurs at the midpoint of the line, if we have supposed the two motions equal in speed and slowness. And it (the midpoint) is the juncture of the third and fourth of those parts, relative to each end of the line, as is clear with the slightest sincere reflection.  He said: (We suppose a line, etc.) The proponents of the part say: its supposed boundary is like the suppositions in the Ta'limat, and we do not accept its existence externally.  He said: (Indeed the one that meets from one of the two parts) The word 'min' here is partitive.
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage gives the second proof, using a line composed of an even number of indivisible parts (e.g., six). Two additional parts are placed above one end and below the other end of the line. They are moved towards each other at equal speeds. When they meet, they will align at the midpoint of the line. This midpoint is precisely the juncture between the third and fourth parts from each end. If an indivisible part were placed exactly at that juncture, it would have to touch both the third and fourth parts, requiring it to be divided. Thus, the indivisible part is shown to be divisible. The marginal comments note that atomists reject the external reality of such a constructed line, comparing it to fictional suppositions in the Ta'limat (a reference to Ibn Sina's work), and provide a grammatical note about the partitive 'min'.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof depends on the actual physical existence of the constructed line and moving parts.
Correction: The proof is a logical thought experiment. It does not require that such a line exists in reality; it only demonstrates a contradiction in the concept of indivisible parts.

A) It must be the same size as the other parts.

C) It must remain stationary.
D) It must be larger than the other parts.

Key Takeaway
The thought experiment shows that if an indivisible part existed at the midpoint, it would have to be divisible because it touches two separate parts.

Proof from a Line of Odd Parts
Al-Iji presents a proof using a line of odd parts and an objection regarding spatial capacity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Iji's thought experiment with a line of odd parts aims to prove that indivisible parts cannot meet without implying division, and the objection that spatial constraints prevent simultaneous occupation of the middle part.

Core Idea
A line of odd parts forces the middle part to become a juncture, revealing the inconsistency of indivisible parts.

In this third proof, al-Iji constructs a thought experiment with a line made of an odd number of indivisible parts. By setting two parts in motion from opposite ends, he aims to show that they must meet in the middle, making that middle part a point of contact. However, an objection questions whether two parts can simultaneously occupy the same middle part, given its indivisibility. This lesson unpacks the proof and the objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the logical structure: setup, motion, meeting point, objection.
The passage uses a hypothetical line of five parts as an example.
2
Visualize the experiment
Imagine a line composed of five indivisible parts. Two parts are placed at the two ends. They move toward each other at equal speed.
3
Understand the meeting point
Since the motion is equal and the number of parts is odd, the two moving parts meet at the third part (the middle). This makes the third part a juncture where both are simultaneously present.
This meeting would imply that the middle part is divisible, as it hosts two parts.
4
Consider the objection
The objection argues that the two moving parts cannot land on the middle part together because it can only accommodate one part. They would need to meet before reaching it, but that would require a part smaller than an indivisible part.
This objection challenges the proof's conclusion by highlighting spatial constraints.
5
Reflect on the significance
This proof is part of a larger debate on the existence of indivisible parts (atoms). It shows the logical difficulties in assuming their existence.
The objection is noted but not refuted in this passage; it may be addressed elsewhere.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : ( منها ( نفرض خطاً من أجزاء وتر كالخمسة مثلاً ) ونفرض ذينك الجزأين كليهما من فوق كلا منهما ( من طرف ( من طرفي الخط ( ثم) نفرض أنهما ) يتحركان ) أي كل منهما إلى صاحبه حركة ( سواء فيلتقيان ) لا محالة ( في الوسط، وهو الجزء الثالث ) من كل واحد من الطرفين ( فيكون هو ) أي الجزء الثالث ( على ملتقاهما ) لأنهما معاً عليه ( وربما يمنع هذا بأنهما ) أي الجزئين المتحركين ( يقعان قبل ) الجزء ( الثالث إذ شرط انتقالهما ) إلى الثالث ( فراغ ما يسع الجزأين معاً، ولا ) شك أن الثالث لا يسعهما بل يسع واحدا منهما .
The third: (Among them) We suppose a line of odd parts, e.g., five. And we suppose those two parts both from above, each from one end of the line. Then we suppose that they move, i.e., each to its companion, with equal motion, so they inevitably meet in the middle, which is the third part from each of the two ends. Thus it (the third part) is at their juncture, because they are both upon it. And perhaps this is refuted by [the argument] that the two moving parts fall before the third part, since the condition of their transition to the third is a vacancy that can accommodate both parts together, and there is no doubt that the third does not accommodate them but accommodates only one of them.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
The third proof uses a line composed of an odd number of indivisible parts, for example five. Two parts are placed above the two ends of the line. They move toward each other at equal speed. They will inevitably meet in the middle, which is the third part from each end. That middle part therefore becomes a juncture where both moving parts meet. This implies that the third part is being touched by two parts simultaneously, which would require it to be divisible. The passage then mentions an objection: the two moving parts would need to occupy the same space as the middle part simultaneously, but the middle part can only hold one part, so they would meet before reaching it. This objection is noted but not answered here; the refutation is implied in subsequent discussion.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the meeting occurs exactly at the middle part without any problem, but the objection shows that the middle part cannot hold two parts simultaneously.
Correction: The objection highlights that if the middle part is truly indivisible, it cannot be occupied by two parts at once, so the moving parts would need to meet before reaching it, creating a dilemma.
