المرصد الأول - النوع الثاني : القوة المدركة الباطنة ( الثاني : القطرة النازلة نراها خطاً) مستقيماً ( والشعلة التي تدار بسرعة شديدة نراها كالدائرة وليستا ) أي القطرة والشعلة ( في الخارج عن القوى المدركة ( خطاً ودائرة فهو أي كونهما كذلك إنما يكون ( في الحس المشترك وليس في الباصرة لأنها إنما تدرك الشيء حيث هو ) حتى إذا زال عن مكانه لم تدركه فيه بل في مكان آخر فقط ( فهو لارتسامهما على الوجه المذكور ( في قوة أخرى سوى الباصرة، ( وليست ) تلك القوة ( هي النفس ( الناطقة لاستحالة اتصافها بما له مقدار ( فهي قوة جسمانية ) باطنة ترتسم فيها صور المحسوسات . ( ولقائل أن يقول : يجوز أن يكون ذلك لارتسامه في القوة (الباصرة وما ذكرتموه من أن الباصرة لا تدرك الشيء إلا حيث هو ممنوع ، إذ لا دليل عليه سوى الاستقراء الذي لا يفيد اليقين. فنقول : لم لا يجوز أن ينطبع في الباصرة صورة الجسم في حيز وقبل أن تنمحى هذه الصورة عنها تنطبع فيها صورته في حيز آخر ، وإذا اجتمعت الصورتان في الباصرة شعرت بهما معاً على أنهما صورة واحدة لشيء واحد ممتد على الاستقامة أو الاستدارة، ويؤيد ذلك أن ابن سينا يسلم أن البصر يدرك الحركة ويستحيل إدراكها إلا على الوجه الذي صورناه، وأيضاً ارتسام ما له امتداد في النفس إنما يستحيل إذا كان حلول الصور فيها كحلول الأعراض في محالها وهو مما ينازع فيه لأن الأعراض متمانعة دون الصور. الوجه ( الثالث : ما يراه النائم والمبرسم والكاهن (موجود فإن كل واحد منهم يشاهد صوراً محسوسة ويدرك أصواتا مسموعة بحيث لا يرتاب فيها ويميز بينها وبين غيرها فلا بد أن يكون لتلك الصور والأصوات وجود إذ العدم المحض يستحيل أن يتميز عن غيره ويشاهد على حسب ما تشاهد الأمور الموجودة ( وليس) وجودها (في الخارج وإلا رآها كل سليم الحس فهو في المدرك (وهو) أي ذلك المدرك ( جسماني) لا عقلي ( لما مر ) . من أن الجزئيات لا تدركها إلاقوى جسمانية وليس حساً ظاهراً لتعطله في النوم ولأن الرائي ربما كان مغموض العينين فوجب أن يكون هناك قوة أخرى ترتسم فيها تلك الصور عند كون المادة غائبة. قلنا : ذلك ممنوع أيضاً إذ لا یری الناقصة من نظر إلى الشمس نظراً في زمان ممتداً وإلى روضة مخضرة ساعة طويلة كما مر في صدر النوع الأول، فإن تلك القصة تدل على أن في الحواس الظاهرة نوعاً آخر للارتسام بحيث لا يقتضي حضور الماهية وإنه يجوز أن يرتسم في الحواس الظاهرة صور لا منه قبل المحسوس أصلاً كما ارتسم في الحس المشترك على ما زعمتم ولا بد لنفي ذلك من دليل . قوله: (وأيضا ) إشارة إلى أنه يجوز أن يكون ذلك لارتسامه في النفس المجردة. وقوله : لأن الأعراض ممانعة دون الصور فإن حصول السواد في محل ينافي حصول البياض في ذلك المحل مع أنه لا يتصور التنافي بين صورتيهما أصلاً. حساً باطناً . ( ولقائل أن يقول : لعل المدرك لها النفس كما مر) من أنها تدرك الكلي والجزئي أيضا وامتناع ارتسام الصور التي لها مقدار فيها غير مسلم عندنا لما عرفت آنفاً ، ( واحتج الخصم ) النافي للحس المشترك (بوجهين : الأول: أن حصول جبل من ياقوت وبحر من زئبق ) كما يرى في النوم ( في جزء من بدن النائم ضروري البطلان قلنا : قد ينطبع شبح الكبير في الصغير) إنما الممتنع أن يرتسم عين الكبير في الصغير ( كما مر الثاني : كما نعلم أنا لا نشم الروائح ( ولا نذوق ) الطعوم ( ولا نسمع الأصوات ) ولا نبصر الألوان ( بالأيدي والأرجل ) ، كذلك ( نعلم أنا لا نذوق ولا نلمس ولا نعقل شيئاً مما ذكرناه ( بالدماغ، ومنكره مكابر لإنكار ما يجده كل عاقل من نفسه . ( قلنا : عدم توسط الدماغ فيه ) أي في الإدراك الحسي ( ممنوع) وما ذكرتموه لا يدل عليه ( وأما أنه ( أي الدماغ ( ليس آلة جرمية ) أي ليس جرمه آلة للإحساسات المذكورة كما اقتضاه دليلكم . ( فنعم ) إذ لا نزاع لنا فيه .
The First Observatory - Second Type: The Internal Perceptive Faculty. (Second: The falling drop we see as a straight line, and the flame that is whirled quickly we see as a circle, and they are not) i.e. the drop and flame (in the external sensible things a line and a circle; rather, their being so is only) in the common sense, not in the sight, because the sight perceives a thing only where it is, so when it departs from its place it does not perceive it there but only in another place. (Thus it is because of their impression in the mentioned manner) in another faculty besides the sight, (and that faculty is not the rational soul) because it is impossible for it to be qualified by what has magnitude; (so it is a bodily faculty) internal, in which the forms of sensed things are impressed. (And an objector may say: It is possible that this is due to its impression in the faculty of sight, and what you mentioned that the sight perceives a thing only where it is is not conceded, for there is no proof for it except induction, which does not yield certainty. So we say: Why is it not permissible that the image of a body is impressed in the sight in one location, and before this image is erased from it, its image is impressed in it in another location, and when the two images are combined in the sight, it perceives them together as a single image of a single thing extended straightly or circularly? And this is supported by the fact that Ibn Sina concedes that the eye perceives motion, and it is impossible to perceive it except in the manner we have depicted. Also, the impression of what has extension in the soul is impossible only if the indwelling of forms in it is like the indwelling of accidents in their loci, which is disputed because accidents are mutually exclusive, unlike forms.) (The Third Argument: What the sleeper, the feverish person, and the soothsayer see) are existent, for each one of them observes sensible forms and hears audible sounds such that he does not doubt them and distinguishes them from others. So inevitably those forms and sounds have existence, for pure non-existence cannot be distinguished from others and be observed as observed existent things are observed. (And their existence is not) in the outside world; otherwise every sound-sensed person would see them. So it is in a perceiver, (and that perceiver is bodily), not intellectual, (as has passed): because particulars are perceived only by bodily faculties, and it is not an external sense because it becomes inactive during sleep, and because the seer may be with eyes shut. So there must be another faculty in which those forms are impressed when the matter is absent. (We said: That too is not conceded, because) one who looks at the sun for an extended period and at a green meadow for a long time does not see the afterimage as we passed at the beginning of the first type. For that story indicates that in the external senses there is another kind of impression that does not require the presence of the quiddity, and it is permissible that images are impressed in the external senses that have no source from the sensed thing at all, just as they are impressed in the common sense according to your claim, and proving its impossibility requires evidence.) (His saying: 'Also' is an indication that it is possible that this is due to its impression in the abstract soul. And his saying: 'Because accidents are mutually exclusive, unlike forms': for the occurrence of blackness in a locus prevents the occurrence of whiteness in that same locus, while conflict between their two forms is not conceivable at all.) an internal sense. (And an objector may say: Perhaps the perceiver of them is the soul, as has passed) that it perceives both the universal and the particular, and the impossibility of impression of forms that have magnitude in it is not admitted by us, as you have known earlier. (And the opponent, the one who denies the common sense, argued with two proofs: The first: That a mountain of ruby and a sea of mercury as seen in sleep occur in a part of the sleeper's body is necessarily false. We said: A phantom of the large may be impressed in the small; it is only impossible that the exact large itself is impressed in the small, as has passed. The second: Just as we know that we do not smell odors, taste tastes, hear sounds, or see colors with hands and feet, so we know that we do not taste, touch, or reason anything of what we mentioned with the brain; and whoever denies this is obstinate in denying what every rational person finds in himself. We said: The non-intermediacy of the brain in that, i.e., in sensory perception, is not conceded, and what you mentioned does not prove it. As for that it (the brain) is not a bodily instrument, i.e., its substance is not an instrument for the mentioned sensations as your proof required, then yes, for there is no dispute with us about it.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage is a sustained argument for the existence and nature of the common sense (al-ḥiss al-mushtarak), an internal bodily faculty. The author begins by giving two examples: a falling drop seen as a continuous line, and a rapidly whirled flame seen as a circle. These perceptions, he argues, are not in the external sense of sight (al-bāṣira), because sight perceives an object only at its current location; when the object moves, sight does not retain the previous location. Instead, the composite impression must occur in a different power—the common sense—which is a bodily faculty, not the rational soul (al-nafs al-nāṭiqa), because the soul cannot receive extended images. Next, an objector raises two points: first, the image might be impressed in the sight itself through successive impressions that blend, and second, the soul might receive extended images if forms are not like accidents. The author responds (فنقول) that the first is possible only if the sight retains the first image while the second impresses—this is exactly what the common sense does; the second fails because the objection misunderstands the nature of forms versus accidents. Then the author presents a third argument from dreams and visions: the sleeper, feverish person, and soothsayer perceive real-seeming images and sounds, which cannot be pure non-existence. These are not external, otherwise everyone would see them, so they must be in an internal bodily faculty (since particulars require a bodily perceiver) that is not an external sense (as it works during sleep or with closed eyes). An objector immediately challenges this: the afterimage from looking at the sun shows that external senses can retain images without the object present, so perhaps dream images are also in external senses. The author retorts that this does not disprove the need for an internal sense; indeed, the afterimage itself indicates another type of impression, which could be in the external sense only if it were like the common sense, but the objector must provide evidence. The objector also suggests the soul could perceive them. The author notes that the soul's inability to receive extended forms is not conceded. Finally, the opponent (al-khaṣm) who denies the common sense altogether brings two proofs: (1) A mountain of ruby and sea of mercury seen in sleep cannot physically exist in a part of the body; the author answers that only a phantom (shabah) of the large can fit in the small, not the exact object. (2) Just as we know we do not smell with hands, we know we do not perceive with the brain; the author responds that the brain's non-involvement in perception is not conceded, and their proof does not establish it; they agree only that the brain's substance is not a direct instrument for sensation, which is not disputed.

Key Terms
الحس المشترك
النفس الناطقة
ارتسام الصور
المدرك الجسماني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Dream images are purely imaginary with no real substrate.
Correction: The author argues that dream images must have real existence in a faculty, otherwise they could not be distinguished.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why cannot dream images be purely non-existent?
A) Because non-existence cannot be distinguished or observed like existent things.
B) Because they are seen by many people.
C) Because they have a physical source.
D) Because the sleeper is conscious.

Key Takeaway
Dreams and visions demonstrate that there must be an internal bodily sense that receives and stores sensory images independent of external objects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever had a dream so vivid that it felt completely real? How does this lesson challenge the way you view the reality of those dream experiences? What does that mean for your understanding of your own inner senses? Commit to paying attention to your dreams tonight and reflecting on where they come from.
?
If the common sense is a bodily faculty that stores images, and it remains active during sleep, how does that affect your view of the soul's role? Are you willing to accept that your deepest perceptions might be tied to your physical body, not just your soul? What change in your daily spiritual practice does that demand?
?
The afterimage objection shows that external senses can retain impressions. But Sanusi insists an internal sense is still needed. Do you ever mistake a temporary sensation for a permanent reality? How can this distinction between external and internal perception guide you to discern truth from illusion in your faith? What specific step will you take this week to verify the source of a strong impression?

Rational Judgments
intermediate
6 min
Refuting the Opponent's Two Proofs Against the Common Sense
Al-Isfahani responds to two final objections against the common sense.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Isfahani refutes two key objections that deny the existence of the common sense.

Core Idea
Physical impossibility and brain-based arguments are effectively countered by distinguishing phantom from real and challenging the proof.

Having presented his own proofs, al-Isfahani now turns to address two arguments made by those who deny the common sense altogether. The first argues that impossible physical objects like a mountain of ruby cannot fit in a small body part; the second claims that the brain cannot be involved in sensory perception because we know we do not perceive with it. This lesson shows how the author answers both challenges.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the final part of the source, which contains two objections and their refutations.
Notice the structure: objection, response for both arguments.
2
Understand the first objection
The opponent argues that a mountain of ruby and sea of mercury cannot physically exist in a small part of the body; thus the common sense cannot be bodily.
3
Understand the author's response
The author replies that only a phantom of the large is impressed, not the actual large object; this is not impossible.
The key distinction: image vs. real object.
4
Understand the second objection
The opponent claims we know we do not perceive with the brain, just as we know we do not smell with hands.
5
Understand the author's response
The author says the non-intermediacy of the brain is not proven; the opponent only shows it is not a direct instrument, which is not disputed.
The brain may still be an intermediary via the common sense.

The First Observatory - Second Type: The Internal Perceptive Faculty. (Second: The falling drop we see as a straight line, and the flame that is whirled quickly we see as a circle, and they are not) i.e. the drop and flame (in the external sensible things a line and a circle; rather, their being so is only) in the common sense, not in the sight, because the sight perceives a thing only where it is, so when it departs from its place it does not perceive it there but only in another place. (Thus it is because of their impression in the mentioned manner) in another faculty besides the sight, (and that faculty is not the rational soul) because it is impossible for it to be qualified by what has magnitude; (so it is a bodily faculty) internal, in which the forms of sensed things are impressed. (And an objector may say: It is possible that this is due to its impression in the faculty of sight, and what you mentioned that the sight perceives a thing only where it is is not conceded, for there is no proof for it except induction, which does not yield certainty. So we say: Why is it not permissible that the image of a body is impressed in the sight in one location, and before this image is erased from it, its image is impressed in it in another location, and when the two images are combined in the sight, it perceives them together as a single image of a single thing extended straightly or circularly? And this is supported by the fact that Ibn Sina concedes that the eye perceives motion, and it is impossible to perceive it except in the manner we have depicted. Also, the impression of what has extension in the soul is impossible only if the indwelling of forms in it is like the indwelling of accidents in their loci, which is disputed because accidents are mutually exclusive, unlike forms.) (The Third Argument: What the sleeper, the feverish person, and the soothsayer see) are existent, for each one of them observes sensible forms and hears audible sounds such that he does not doubt them and distinguishes them from others. So inevitably those forms and sounds have existence, for pure non-existence cannot be distinguished from others and be observed as observed existent things are observed. (And their existence is not) in the outside world; otherwise every sound-sensed person would see them. So it is in a perceiver, (and that perceiver is bodily), not intellectual, (as has passed): because particulars are perceived only by bodily faculties, and it is not an external sense because it becomes inactive during sleep, and because the seer may be with eyes shut. So there must be another faculty in which those forms are impressed when the matter is absent. (We said: That too is not conceded, because) one who looks at the sun for an extended period and at a green meadow for a long time does not see the afterimage as we passed at the beginning of the first type. For that story indicates that in the external senses there is another kind of impression that does not require the presence of the quiddity, and it is permissible that images are impressed in the external senses that have no source from the sensed thing at all, just as they are impressed in the common sense according to your claim, and proving its impossibility requires evidence.) (His saying: 'Also' is an indication that it is possible that this is due to its impression in the abstract soul. And his saying: 'Because accidents are mutually exclusive, unlike forms': for the occurrence of blackness in a locus prevents the occurrence of whiteness in that same locus, while conflict between their two forms is not conceivable at all.) an internal sense. (And an objector may say: Perhaps the perceiver of them is the soul, as has passed) that it perceives both the universal and the particular, and the impossibility of impression of forms that have magnitude in it is not admitted by us, as you have known earlier. (And the opponent, the one who denies the common sense, argued with two proofs: The first: That a mountain of ruby and a sea of mercury as seen in sleep occur in a part of the sleeper's body is necessarily false. We said: A phantom of the large may be impressed in the small; it is only impossible that the exact large itself is impressed in the small, as has passed. The second: Just as we know that we do not smell odors, taste tastes, hear sounds, or see colors with hands and feet, so we know that we do not taste, touch, or reason anything of what we mentioned with the brain; and whoever denies this is obstinate in denying what every rational person finds in himself. We said: The non-intermediacy of the brain in that, i.e., in sensory perception, is not conceded, and what you mentioned does not prove it. As for that it (the brain) is not a bodily instrument, i.e., its substance is not an instrument for the mentioned sensations as your proof required, then yes, for there is no dispute with us about it.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage is a sustained argument for the existence and nature of the common sense (al-ḥiss al-mushtarak), an internal bodily faculty. The author begins by giving two examples: a falling drop seen as a continuous line, and a rapidly whirled flame seen as a circle. These perceptions, he argues, are not in the external sense of sight (al-bāṣira), because sight perceives an object only at its current location; when the object moves, sight does not retain the previous location. Instead, the composite impression must occur in a different power—the common sense—which is a bodily faculty, not the rational soul (al-nafs al-nāṭiqa), because the soul cannot receive extended images. Next, an objector raises two points: first, the image might be impressed in the sight itself through successive impressions that blend, and second, the soul might receive extended images if forms are not like accidents. The author responds (فنقول) that the first is possible only if the sight retains the first image while the second impresses—this is exactly what the common sense does; the second fails because the objection misunderstands the nature of forms versus accidents. Then the author presents a third argument from dreams and visions: the sleeper, feverish person, and soothsayer perceive real-seeming images and sounds, which cannot be pure non-existence. These are not external, otherwise everyone would see them, so they must be in an internal bodily faculty (since particulars require a bodily perceiver) that is not an external sense (as it works during sleep or with closed eyes). An objector immediately challenges this: the afterimage from looking at the sun shows that external senses can retain images without the object present, so perhaps dream images are also in external senses. The author retorts that this does not disprove the need for an internal sense; indeed, the afterimage itself indicates another type of impression, which could be in the external sense only if it were like the common sense, but the objector must provide evidence. The objector also suggests the soul could perceive them. The author notes that the soul's inability to receive extended forms is not conceded. Finally, the opponent (al-khaṣm) who denies the common sense altogether brings two proofs: (1) A mountain of ruby and sea of mercury seen in sleep cannot physically exist in a part of the body; the author answers that only a phantom (shabah) of the large can fit in the small, not the exact object. (2) Just as we know we do not smell with hands, we know we do not perceive with the brain; the author responds that the brain's non-involvement in perception is not conceded, and their proof does not establish it; they agree only that the brain's substance is not a direct instrument for sensation, which is not disputed.

Key Terms
الحس المشترك
شبح
آلة جرمية
الدماغ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The common sense must be a physical organ like the brain.
Correction: The common sense is a bodily faculty, but the brain may be an instrument; the author does not specify its location.

Checkpoint
How does the author respond to the first objection that a mountain of ruby cannot fit in a small body part?
A) He denies that such dreams occur.
B) He argues that only a phantom of the large is impressed, not the actual object.
C) He claims the mountain is not actually ruby.
D) He says the dream is not real.

Key Takeaway
Both physical and epistemic objections to the common sense can be answered: impressions do not require physical containment, and the brain's role is not disproven.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever limited God's power or the reality of the unseen by thinking that if something cannot be physically contained, it cannot exist? This lesson shows that an image of a mountain can exist in a small part of the body. How might you be restricting your faith by materialistic assumptions?
?
The opponent claimed that we know we do not perceive with the brain because it is obvious. But the author challenges this assumption. Is there a belief you hold about your own soul or intellect that you have never questioned because it seems too obvious? Will you examine it today?
?
When you reflect on your dreams or imagination, do you consider them as 'real' in a non-physical sense? How does understanding that a phantom can represent what is impossible physically change how you view the spiritual world?

The Faculty of Imagination: Definition and Necessity
Al-Isfahani defines imagination and argues for its necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of imagination (al-khayāl) as the preserver of forms received by common sense and why this faculty is necessary for recognition and orderly life.

Core Idea
Imagination preserves sensory forms after they are absent, enabling recognition and coherence in human affairs.

Al-Isfahani introduces the second internal perceptive faculty, imagination. He defines it as the preserver of forms first received by common sense, likening it to a storehouse. Without this faculty, recognition of previously seen objects would be impossible, leading to chaos in daily life and even the afterlife. This lesson establishes the conceptual foundation for understanding imagination's role.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Read the Arabic passage defining imagination as the preserver of forms. Notice the storehouse analogy.
The term 'al-khayāl' is often translated as 'imagination' but here refers to a memory-like faculty.
2
Understand the necessity argument
Consider the example of recognizing a person seen before. The passage explains that without imagination, recognition would be impossible, causing confusion between beneficial and harmful.
This shows that imagination is essential for practical and religious order.
3
Connect to internal senses
Place imagination within the scheme of internal perceptive faculties. It complements common sense (reception) by preserving what is received.

The second of the internal perceptive faculties: the imagination (al-khayāl), which preserves the forms impressed in the common sense when the sensed objects are absent from the external senses. It is like a storehouse for it. And by it one recognizes whom one saw at one time, then disappears, then reappears. Were it not for this power and its preservation of the forms of absent sensed objects, recognition would be impossible—that is, it would be impossible to know of a thing that it was what was seen previously in time—and order would be disrupted. For then a person would need in every thing he senses to learn its state the second time and thereafter as in the first time; thus harmful would not be distinguished from beneficial, nor friend from enemy, and the affairs of livelihood and the afterlife would be disrupted. (He proved) the existence of the imagination by three proofs. The first: The power of reception is not the power of preservation; thus the perceiver of forms that receive them—namely the common sense—is not their preserver, which is the imagination. (We said: What you hold to is a branch of your statement that the one does not issue from it except one, and its falsity has passed. And even if that is conceded, preservation is conditioned by reception as a matter of course; so reception must be combined with preservation. So how do you say that the receiver is not the preserver at all, in order to prove that the perceiver of sensed things must be distinct from that which preserves them?) The second: The common sense judges the sensed things, as has preceded, unlike the faculty of imagination, because its act is preservation, and there is no doubt that what is not a judge is distinct from what is a judge. (We said: It is possible that there is a single power that sometimes judges and sometimes does not judge; thus only a distinction in consideration is required, not in essence.) The third: Sensed forms, when they are impressed in the common sense, are observed, as in the case of present sensed things with us, unlike when they are impressed in the imagination, for then they are not so. For when sensed things are absent from us, there must be a difference between the two powers in terms of essence. (We said: What you mentioned of difference may return...
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage defines the imagination (al-khayāl) as the second internal perceptive faculty. Its function is to preserve the forms that were first received by the common sense, acting as a storehouse. This preservation enables recognition of previously perceived objects; without it, one could not distinguish a familiar thing from a new one, causing chaos in practical and religious affairs. The author then presents three proofs that the imagination is a distinct faculty from the common sense, but each proof is followed by a refutation. The first proof argues that reception and preservation are different powers, so the common sense (receiver) and imagination (preserver) must be distinct. The response counters that this presupposes the principle that one thing cannot produce two effects, which is already refuted; moreover, preservation necessarily presupposes reception, so they can coexist in one faculty. The second proof says the common sense judges (ḥākim) while the imagination merely preserves, and a judge cannot be the same as a non-judge. The response: a single power can perform different acts at different times, so the distinction is only conceptual, not essential. The third proof observes that when forms are in the common sense, they are perceived as present (as in immediate sensation), but when in the imagination, they are not perceived as present (as in memory). Therefore the two powers must differ in essence. The response is cut off, but it likely argues that this difference in perception does not necessarily entail two distinct essences (e.g., the same faculty may perceive differently depending on attention or conditions). The discussion remains open.

الخيال
الحس المشترك
قوة الحفظ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary function of the imagination (al-khayāl) according to this passage?
A) To receive new sensory data from the external senses.
B) To preserve forms of sensed objects after they are absent.
C) To judge the truth of sensory perceptions.
D) To create new forms from imagination alone.

Key Takeaway
Imagination is the faculty that preserves sensory forms for later recognition, and without it, coherent thought and life are impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider a person you saw today and recognized. Without imagination, you would have no idea who they are. How does this daily, unnoticed faculty remind you of Allah's precise design? Will you thank Him for it now?
?
Imagine the chaos described in the passage—inability to distinguish friend from foe, beneficial from harmful. In what areas of your life have you failed to 'preserve' the right knowledge, leading to confusion? What step will you take to safeguard that knowledge?
?
The passage links imagination to both worldly and religious order. Is your imagination—what you store and recall—serving your afterlife? Are there images or memories you should consciously replace with something better? Commit to one change today.

The Three Proofs for the Existence of Imagination
Al-Isfahani presents three proofs for the distinction of imagination and their refutations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three rational proofs Al-Isfahani presents to show that imagination is a distinct faculty from common sense, and the refutations of each proof.

Core Idea
Three proofs attempt to distinguish imagination from common sense, but each is rebutted, suggesting they may be the same faculty with different functions.

Al-Isfahani now presents three proofs intended to demonstrate that the imagination is a separate faculty from the common sense. Each proof is based on a different aspect: reception vs. preservation, the act of judging vs. non-judging, and the mode of perception (present vs. absent). After each proof, an opponent's objection is given, challenging the reasoning. This lesson walks through this dialectical structure, showing how the proofs are both presented and criticized.

Guided Path
1
Enumerate the three proofs
Identify the three proofs listed: (1) based on reception vs. preservation, (2) based on judgment vs. non-judgment, (3) based on present vs. absent perception.
Each proof follows the pattern: statement of distinction, then objection starting with 'قلنا'.
2
Examine the first proof and its refutation
The first proof argues that reception and preservation are different powers, so common sense (receiver) and imagination (preserver) must be distinct. The refutation says this relies on the principle 'one cause produces one effect,' which is false; moreover preservation requires reception, so they can coexist.
The refutation uses a dialectical style: first attacking the underlying principle, then conceding but showing the conclusion does not follow.
3
Examine the second proof and its refutation
The second proof says common sense judges while imagination does not, so they are distinct. The refutation says one faculty can judge sometimes and not others; the distinction is conceptual, not essential.
4
Examine the third proof and its refutation
The third proof notes that forms in common sense are perceived as present, but in imagination they are not; therefore the faculties differ in essence. The refutation is cut off but likely argues that the same faculty can perceive differently under different conditions.
The passage ends mid-sentence; the full refutation is not provided in this excerpt.
5
Reflect on the overall argument
Consider the cumulative effect: the proofs are not decisive, leaving open the possibility that imagination and common sense are one faculty with two aspects.
This reflects the Ash‘ari approach of preserving simplicity in the soul.

?

Why Atomists Include Accidents in the Body's Reality
The note clarifies that atomists rule accidents into the body's essence to avoid circularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the atomists ruled that accidents are included in the reality of bodies and how this avoids circularity.

Core Idea
To avoid circularity, the atomists ruled accidents part of a body's reality.

This marginal note tackles a subtle logical issue in atomist doctrine. The atomists held that individual atoms are homogeneous, yet bodies differ. To explain this difference without falling into circular reasoning, they had to consider accidents as part of the very reality (ḥaqīqa) of bodies. This passage clarifies that distinction between a ruling and ontological inclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the main argument.
Notice the key terms: أعراض, حقيقة, دور.
2
Understand the problem of circularity
If accidents were truly part of the essence, circularity would follow. The note distinguishes a ruling from actual ontological inclusion.
3
Identify the atomists' solution
Because bodies have different realities while atoms are homogeneous, atomists are forced to rule accidents as part of reality to account for diversity.
4
Examine the interpretive concession
The note offers a possible interpretation: atoms are the greatest pillar, so they are loosely called the reality as a concession.
Musāmaḥa (مسامحة) means leniency or concession.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بجعل الأعراض داخلة في حقيقة الجسم هو الحكم بكون الأعراض داخلة في حقيقته لا كونها داخلة في حقيقته في نفس الأمر حتى يلزم الدور، فإنه لما كانت الأجسام متخالفة الحقائق أن الجواهر الفردة متجانسة عندهم لزمهم أن يحكموا بأن الأعراض داخلة في حقائها. وقوله : فيكون منافياً لما أجمعوا عليه إلخ. ويمكن تأويل كلامهم بأنهم لما رأوا أن الجواهر الفردة هي الركن الأعظم للجسم وأن الأعراض تابعة لها جعلوا الجواهر الفردة حقائق بالضرورة مع الأجسام مسامحة منهم في ذلك .
By making the accidents included in the reality of the body is the ruling that the accidents are included in its reality, not that they are included in its reality in itself such that circularity would follow. For since bodies have different realities while the individual atoms are homogeneous in their view, they were obliged to rule that the accidents are included in their realities. And his statement: 'It would be contrary to what they have all agreed upon' etc. And it is possible to interpret their statement by saying that since they saw that individual atoms are the greatest pillar of the body and that accidents are subordinate to them, they made the individual atoms realities necessarily along with bodies, as a concession from them in that.
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This marginal note addresses a subtle point in atomist doctrine concerning whether accidents are included in the reality (ḥaqīqa) of a body. The note distinguishes between a ruling (ḥukm) that accidents are included and an actual ontological inclusion. If the latter were true, it would lead to circularity (dawr). The atomists, who hold that individual atoms are homogeneous yet bodies have different realities, are forced to rule that accidents are part of the realities of bodies to account for the differences. The note then offers an interpretation (taʾwīl) of the atomists' position: since they consider individual atoms the greatest pillar (rukn aʿẓam) of the body and accidents as secondary, they may have loosely (musāmaḥa) treated the atoms as the realities of bodies themselves. The note helps clarify the reasoning behind the atomist view and shows how it can be understood without logical contradiction.

Key Terms
الأعراض
حقيقة الجسم
الجواهر الفردة
الدور
مسامحة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the atomists claimed accidents are ontologically part of the body's essence.
Correction: The note clarifies that it is a ruling (ḥukm) to avoid circularity, not an ontological claim.

Checkpoint
Why does the note say the atomists were obliged to rule that accidents are included in the reality of bodies?
A) Because bodies have different realities while atoms are homogeneous
B) Because atoms cannot exist without accidents
C) Because the Torah says so
D) Because of a proof from the philosophers

Key Takeaway
The atomists' inclusion of accidents in the reality of bodies is a logical ruling to account for diversity, not an ontological claim, to avoid circularity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The atomists adjusted their definition of a body to avoid logical contradiction, even though it required a concession. When have you been forced to refine a conviction or belief to preserve its consistency with deeper principles? What did that cost you, and what did it save?
?
Just as the atomists ruled accidents into the body's reality to account for diversity, do you ever impose categories or explanations on your experiences simply to avoid uncomfortable complexity? Are you willing to sit with circular reasoning in your own life?
?
The atomists distinguished between a ruling and an ontological claim. In your own faith, is there any belief you hold as a 'ruling'—something you affirm for practical consistency but not as absolute truth? How would that be exposed, and what would be at stake?

First Proof: Bodies Are Temporal Because They Cannot Be Free from Temporal Things
Sanusi presents the first proof for temporal origination of bodies with its two premises and the beginning of the first argument for the first premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of the first proof for the temporal origination of bodies: its two premises and how they lead to the conclusion that bodies are temporally originated.

Core Idea
Bodies cannot be eternal because they are always accompanied by temporal things.

Sanusi now begins the substantive demonstration of the temporal origination of bodies. He announces six proofs, and the first and most famous is the argument from the impossibility of being free from temporal things. This passage lays out the core syllogism: bodies are never free from temporal things, and whatever is never free from temporal things is itself temporal. We will examine the premises and see how the argument unfolds.

Guided Path
1
Identify the overall proof structure
Read the passage and note that it contains a syllogism with two premises and a conclusion. The conclusion is that bodies are temporally originated.
This proof is the first of six.
2
Understand the second premise
The text says the second premise is 'apparent' because if something eternal had temporal things, that would entail the eternity of the temporal, which is contradictory.
The full discussion of this premise will come later.
3
Begin exploring the first premise
The first premise is that bodies are never free from temporal things. It is supported by two arguments; the first argument is that bodies are never free from accidents, specifically the categories of space-time (akwan) and composition (ta'lif) and their accompanying accidents.
The justification continues after this passage.

The Sixth Proof: Distinction Through Accidents
Al-Jurjani presents the sixth proof for the origination of bodies based on distinction through accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the sixth proof for the origination of bodies, which argues that bodies cannot exist without being distinguished from one another, and this distinction is only possible through accidents that are themselves originated.

Core Idea
Bodies need accidents to be distinct, and accidents are originated, so bodies are originated.

Al-Jurjani now presents the sixth proof, building on earlier demonstrations that accidents are originated. The core insight is that bodies, composed of similar individual atoms, require accidents to be distinguished from each other. Since accidents are proven originated, the bodies themselves must also be originated. This proof is distinct from the earlier ones because it focuses on the necessity of distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the initial claim that bodies cannot exist without differentiation, and how the author connects this to the nature of atoms and accidents.
The passage is dense and includes repetition from earlier proofs; focus on the opening argument.
2
Identify the key premises
The proof rests on two premises: (1) All bodies must be distinct from each other. (2) Distinction is only possible through accidents. Since accidents are already proven originated, bodies are also originated.
3
Reflect on the common misunderstanding
The common misunderstanding is confusing this proof with the first proof. The first proof argues that bodies cannot exist without accidents; this proof argues that bodies cannot be distinct without accidents.
The passage also repeats the second aspect about motion and rest, but that is a recap of earlier material.

The second observatory – the first goal: originated bodies are four divisions. The sixth of this observatory: And since bodies do not exist without differentiation between them, because every existent must necessarily be distinguished from another existent, and we have shown that differentiation between bodies is only through accidents, based on the similarity of the individual atoms from which bodies are composed; then accidents are originated because they do not endure for two moments, and everything that is like that is originated; and their proof has already passed—i.e., the proof that differentiation between bodies is only through accidents, and the proof that accidents do not endure for two moments—and if he had confined himself to mentioning the proof of the second it would have been better because he said: 'And we have shown.' The second of the two aspects: it is said: the body is not free from motion and rest, and they are two originated things; so the body is not free from accidents. We only say that the body is not free from them because it is not free from being in a place by necessity. If its being in that place is preceded by a being (i.e., another being) in that place, then it is at rest, because rest is the second being in the first place; otherwise (i.e., if its being in that place is not preceded by a being in it) then it is moving. It cannot be said: your proof is refuted by the body in the first moment of its occurrence, because it applies to it while it is neither moving nor at rest, since it is not then qualified by a second being, neither in the first place nor in the second place. For we say: the discussion is about the remaining body; so it is claimed that it is not free from motion or rest, not about the newly originated body, so there is no refutation. And if this question is posed as a contradiction, it is a rejection that does not harm the one giving the reason, because his purpose is the origination of the body. We only say that motion is originated for several reasons: First: The essence of motion is preceded by the other, i.e., its essence necessitates precedence by its very nature because it is from one state to another; rather we say: it is the second being in another place, so it is preceded by the first state and the first being. And the essence of eternity is non-precedence by the other, and between them is an essential opposition, so motion cannot be eternal, and that is the meaning of the originated. Second: The essence does not exist except in particular instances because the absolute cannot be conceived as existing separately from all determinations; and there is no doubt that none of the particular instances of motion exist in eternity, because each part of it is divisible into parts that cannot be gathered together, so they only exist successively; so its essence also does not exist in it (i.e., in eternity), so its essence is originated like its particulars. Third: Every particular motion is preceded by an eternal non-existence, so the non-existences (i.e., the non-existences of all particular motions) are gathered in
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage is the presentation of the sixth proof for the origination of bodies, though it also repeats some of the second aspect from the first proof. The proof begins with the claim that bodies do not exist without being distinct from one another, and this distinctness is necessarily through accidents, because the individual atoms composing bodies are similar. Accidents themselves are originated because they do not endure for two moments. The author then refers to the previous explanation of these two points: that distinctness is only through accidents, and that accidents do not endure. He notes that it would have been sufficient to mention only the second proof (about accidents not enduring). Then the text repeats the second aspect from the first proof: the body is never free from motion or rest, both of which are originated. This is justified by the body necessarily occupying a place; if it is preceded in the same place, it is rest; otherwise motion. The objection about the first moment is answered by restricting the argument to persistent bodies, not newly originated ones. The passage then lists three reasons for motion being originated: (1) The essence of motion is defined as being preceded by another state, whereas eternity is non-precedence; they are incompatible. (2) The essence of motion exists only in particular instances, and since particular motions cannot exist all at once in eternity, the essence itself is originated. (3) Every particular motion is preceded by an eternal non-existence, so the non-existences are all gathered together (the text cuts off here).

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The sixth proof is essentially the same as the first proof.
Correction: The first proof argues that bodies cannot exist without accidents; the sixth proof argues that bodies cannot be distinct without accidents. They are distinct arguments, though both lead to the origination of bodies.

?

The Correlation Proof for Finite Motions
Al-Iji presents the fifth proof using the method of correlation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the method of correlation (tadhāyuf) demonstrates that an infinite series of motions is impossible because the correlatives 'preceding' and 'preceded' must be equal in number.

Core Idea
Preceding and preceded must be equal, so an infinite series is impossible.

Al-Iji now presents a proof based on the logical relationship between correlatives. He takes a series of motions (days) and argues that if it were infinite, the number of 'preceded' parts would exceed the number of 'preceding' parts by one. But because 'preceding' and 'preceded' are true correlatives, they must be equal in number. This contradiction proves the series must be finite.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own flow come through before you interpret.
Notice that Al-Iji uses a compressed style and assumes familiarity with the method of correlation.
2
Identify the key correlatives
Identify 'preceding' (sābiq) and 'preceded' (masbūq) as the two correlatives. Understand that they are 'real correlatives' (mutadhāyifān ḥaqīqiyān) meaning they come in pairs and must exist in equal number.
The term 'tadhāyuf' refers to the mutual relation between two things where each is defined in terms of the other.
3
Understand the counting argument
Follow the setup: take the present day as the 'last' part. In an infinite chain, every part except the last is both preceding and preceded. The last is only preceded. Thus the count of preceded parts exceeds preceding parts by one.
This imbalance is what contradicts the nature of correlatives.
4
Consider the nuance about father-son
Al-Iji notes that equality of 'well-known' terms (like father and son) is not strictly required unless we make a conceptual distinction. However, in the pure correlative relation, equality holds. This shows the proof is rigorous.
This nuance prevents a potential objection.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The contradiction forces us to reject the hypothesis of an infinite series. Therefore, the series of motions is finite.
This proof is one of several ways to establish that motions are created and limited.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس : من تلك الوجوه طريقة التضايف وقد عرفتها أيضا هناك وتقريرها هنا أن الحركات تتألف من أجزاء بعضها سابقة وبعضها مسبوقة ولنجعلها أياماً مثلاً فلو كانت تلك الأيام غير متناهية أمكن لنا أن نجعل من يوم ما وهو اليوم الذي نحن فيه جزءاً أخيراً فنقول : هذا الجزء في هذه السلسلة التي لا تتناهى مسبوق أي موصوف بالمسبوقية وليس بسابق وكل جزء من أجزائها الأخر سابق ومسبوق بحسب الفرض إذ المفروض لا تناهي السلسلة فكل واحد من أجزائها الأخر موصوف بالمسبوقية والسابقية معاً إذ لو وجد فيها سابق غير موصوف بالمسبوقية لانقطعت السلسلة به وعلى هذا التقدير فكل سابق مسبوق من غير عكس كلي كالأخير المذكور فيكون عدد المسبوق أي المسبوقية أزيد من عدد السابق أي السابقية بواحد وأنه محال لأنهما متضايفان حقيقيان يجب تكافؤهما في الوجود وتساويهما في العدد وأن يكون بإزاء كل واحد من أحدهما واحد من الآخر. وأما تساوي عدد المشهوريين فغير لازم كأب واحد له أبناء إلا أن يعتبر التغاير الاعتباري بحسب الوصف، ولو كانت السلسلة متناهية كان هناك سابق ليس بمسبوق فيتكافأ الإضافيان
The fifth: from those ways is the method of correlation, and you have already known it there. Its formulation here is that motions are composed of parts, some of which are preceding and some preceded. Let us make them, for example, days. So if those days were infinite, it would be possible for us to make from a certain day—which is the day we are in—a final part and we say: this part in this chain which does not terminate is preceded, i.e., described with precededness, and is not preceding, and every other part of its parts is preceding and preceded according to the hypothesis, for the hypothesis is that the chain is not finite, so every one of its other parts is described with both precededness and precedingness together, for if there were found in it a preceding not described with precededness, the chain would be cut off by it. On this estimation, every preceding is preceded without full converse like the mentioned last part, so the number of preceded, i.e., precededness, is greater than the number of preceding, i.e., precedingness, by one, and that is impossible because they are two real correlatives that must be equivalent in existence and equal in number, and that for every one of the one there be one from the other. As for the equality of the number of the two well-known ones, it is not necessary, like one father having sons, unless a conceptual distinction is considered according to description. And if the chain were finite, there would be a preceding not preceded, so the two correlatives would be equivalent.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth proof for the finitude of motions, known as the method of correlation (tadhāyuf). The author sets up an infinite series of motions, here likened to days. He chooses a specific day (the present) as the last part of the series. This last part is described as 'preceded' (masbūq) but not 'preceding' (sābiq). Every other part in the infinite series is both preceded and preceding. Consequently, the total number of preceded items exceeds the number of preceding items by one (since the last is only preceded). However, 'preceded' and 'preceding' are real correlatives (mutadhāyifān ḥaqīqiyān) that must be numerically equal in existence; for every one of the one category there must be exactly one from the other. An inequality is impossible, so the series cannot be infinite. The author adds a nuance: the equality of the two 'well-known' terms is not strictly required if we consider father-son relationships, but that is due to a conceptual distinction; in a purely correlative sense, equality holds. If the series were finite, there would be a first 'preceding' that is not 'preceded', restoring equality. Thus, the motions must be finite.

Key Terms
تضايف
سابق
مسبوق
متضايفان حقيقيان
تكافؤ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that in an infinite series, the number of preceding and preceded parts could be equal, but the proof shows that taking a specific point as last forces an inequality.
Correction: The proof demonstrates that if you pick any point as 'last' in an infinite series, that point is only preceded, causing a numerical imbalance. Since the correlatives must be equal, the series cannot be infinite.

Checkpoint
According to the method of correlation, why can't the series of motions be infinite?
A) Because every part is both preceding and preceded.
B) Because the number of preceded parts exceeds the number of preceding parts by one, contradicting the necessary equality of correlatives.
C) Because the series would have no beginning.
D) Because motions are contingent.

Key Takeaway
The correlation proof shows that an infinite series of motions is impossible because it would violate the necessary numerical equality of the correlatives 'preceding' and 'preceded'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This proof shows that your life is part of a finite series of days. If every day you have lived is preceded and will be followed by a limited number of days, how does that reality change the way you will spend the day you are in right now? Will you live it as if it is just one in an infinite chain, or as a precious, non-repeatable moment?
?
The proof shows that correlatives must be equal; an imbalance is impossible. In your relationship with your Creator, are you living in 'balance'—responding to His blessings with gratitude and obedience? Or have you allowed an imbalance to grow, taking His favors without properly acknowledging them as preceded?
?
If the chain of your actions is finite, then every deed you do today is a 'preceded' act that will be followed by consequences. Are you aware that your current actions are not just isolated but part of a finite series leading to an ultimate judgment? What specific step can you take today to ensure that your account of 'preceding' good deeds outweighs the 'preceded' bad ones?

Second Proof: No Eternal Body
Al-Iji presents a second proof against an eternal body using the necessity of a first 'being' in place.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof against an eternal body, which argues that an eternal body would require either an eternal 'being' in place or an infinite regress of 'beings', both of which are rationally impossible.

Core Idea
An eternal body would demand an impossible first 'being' or an infinite chain.

Al-Iji presents a second, more concise proof that no body can be eternal. The argument focuses on the body's need for a spatial 'being' (kawn) at every moment. It shows that this leads to either an eternal 'being' within the body or an infinite chain, both of which are rationally impossible. This proof sharpens the earlier argument by clearly framing the two exhaustive possibilities.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Pay attention to the logical structure: if an eternal body existed, then two possibilities for its 'being' arise.
The passage is incomplete at the end, but the core argument is clear.
2
Identify the two possibilities
The passage states: either the body has a 'being' not preceded by another (first division), or every 'being' is preceded by another ad infinitum (second division). Locate these in the text.
3
Understand why both are impossible
The first division implies an eternal 'being' within the body, which is impossible because an eternal 'being' cannot cease (contradicting observed change). The second division implies an infinite regress, also impossible. Reflect on why each is rejected.
The author notes the second division needs no further explanation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسلك الثاني وهو لبعض المتأخرين كالاختصار للمسلك الأول أنه لو وجد جسم قديم لزم إما كون واحد قديم وإما أن يكون قبل كل كون كون آخر لا إلى نهاية والتالي باطل بقسميه أما الملازمة فلأنه لا بد للجسم من كون في حيز لكونه متحيزاً بالذات فإن وجد له كون غير مسبوق بآخر أي بكون آخر لزم القسم الأول لأن ذلك الكون يجب أن يكون ثابتاً للجسم القديم على الاستمرار فيكون قديما وإلا أي وإن لم يوجد له كون غير مسبوق بآخر لزم القسم الثاني لأن كل كون له فإنه مسبوق يكون آخر فوجب أن يكون قبل كل كون كون لا إلى نهاية إذ على ذلك التقدير الذي نحن فيه لو وجد كون لا كون قبله لزم خلو الجسم عن الكون وأنت خبير بأن القسم الثاني لا يحتاج إلى هذا البيان لأنه إذا لم يوجد له كون غير مسبوق بآخر كان كل كون له مسبوقاً بكون قبله لا إلى نهاية إنما المحتاج إلى البيان هو القسم الأول، بأن يقال : ذلك الكون الذي ليس مسبوقاً أن يكون مستمراً أزلاً وإلا لزم خلو الجسم عن الكون، نعم لو قيل : إن بمثله يجب
The second path, and it is by some of the later scholars, like an abridgment of the first path: that if an eternal body existed, there would necessarily be either one eternal 'being' or that before every 'being' there is another 'being' without end, and the consequent is false in both divisions. As for the entailment, it is because the body must have a 'being' in a place, due to its being essentially occupying space. So if it has a 'being' not preceded by another, i.e., by another 'being', the first division follows, because that 'being' must be constant for the eternal body continuously, so it is eternal. And if not, i.e., if it does not have a 'being' not preceded by another, the second division follows, because every 'being' of it is preceded by another 'being', so there must be before every 'being' a 'being' without end, since on that estimation which we are considering, if there were a 'being' with no 'being' before it, the body would be empty of 'being'. And you are aware that the second division does not need this explanation, because if it does not have a 'being' not preceded by another, then every 'being' of it is preceded by a 'being' before it without end. Rather, what needs explanation is the first division, by saying: that 'being' which is not preceded must be continuous eternally, otherwise the body would be empty of 'being'. Yes, if it were said that like it must [exist]...
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage is a second proof (al-maslak al-thānī) attributed to some later scholars, summarizing the first proof. It argues that if a body were eternal (qadīm), it must occupy a spatial 'being' (kawn) at every moment. Two possibilities arise: either the body has a 'being' that is not preceded by any other 'being', or every 'being' is preceded by another ad infinitum. The first possibility entails an eternal 'being' that continuously belongs to the body, which is impossible because an eternal existential cannot cease (as shown earlier). The second possibility entails an infinite regress of 'beings', which is also impossible. The author notes that the second division (infinite regress) is obviously absurd and needs no further explanation. The first division requires elaboration: the unpreceded 'being' must be eternally continuous; otherwise, the body would have a moment without 'being', which is impossible for a body that essentially occupies space. The text cuts off, but the argument is clearly that both options are impossible, so no body can be eternal. This proof relies on the impossibility of both an eternal 'being' (due to its ceasing being impossible, contradicting observed motion) and infinite regress.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'being' (kawn) refers to the body itself, but it actually means the body's specific spatial location at a given time.
Correction: The 'being' is not the body but its location. The argument concerns the impossibility of an infinite series of locations or an eternal location for a body.

Checkpoint
According to the second path, if a body is eternal, what are the two possibilities for its 'being' in place?
A) It is either necessary or contingent.
B) It is either eternal or caused.
C) It is either not preceded by another being or preceded by an infinite series.
D) It is either simple or compound.

Key Takeaway
The second path proves that an eternal body is impossible because its necessary spatial 'being' would lead to either an eternal 'being' or an infinite regress, both of which are impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever cling to a possession, relationship, or status as if it will never fade, forgetting that nothing besides Allah is eternal? How does the proof that no eternal body exists challenge you to release your grip on created things?
?
The argument shows that every 'being' (every moment and state you experience) is preceded by another, pointing to a First Cause. When you look at your own existence, do you truly feel the weight that you are not self-sufficient but contingent upon Allah at every instant?
?
If an eternal body is impossible, what does that imply about anything you rely on other than Allah? Is there any created thing you treat as if it will never end? Commit today to mentally reminding yourself that only Allah is eternal, and let that change how you value the temporary world.

The First Proof for the Temporality of Bodies
Sanusi presents the first proof for the temporality of bodies using an infinite regress argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the first proof demonstrates that bodies must be created by ruling out an eternal body through the impossibility of an infinite regress of conditions.

Core Idea
An eternal body would require an infinite chain of conditions—impossible, so bodies are created.

This lesson presents the first of several proofs for the temporality of bodies. The author, al-Sanusi, uses a conditional argument to show that if a body were eternal, it would depend on an eternal condition, leading to an infinite regress that cannot be sustained. The argument forces the conclusion that bodies must have a beginning.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Read the Arabic passage from 'المرصد الثاني' to 'وهو المطلوب'. Then read the translation. Notice the conditional structure.
2
Identify the key conditional
The proof starts with 'if the body is not created (eternal), then the eternal conditioned by it would be more deserving of creation' — understand how this leads to an infinite regress.
The argument is about conditions for emanation.
3
Trace the infinite regress argument
Follow how the author forces an end to the regress at something that must emanate without condition, and how the non-existence of that final entity would entail the non-existence of the Necessary Being, which is impossible.
This shows the original body must be created.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثاني - المقصد الأول : الأجسام المحدثة أربعة أقسام حادثا وإلا لكان القديم المشروط به أولى بالحدوث بل يكون ذلك الشرط أيضاً قديماً ويعود الكلام فيه وفي صدوره عن الواجب هل هو بشرط أو بغير شرط ويلزم الانتهاء إلى ما يجب صدوره عن الواجب بغير شرط دفعاً للتسلسل في الأمور المترتبة الموجودة معاً فلو عدم هذا الصادر المنتهي إليه عدم الواجب هذا خلف فإذا امتنع عدم هذا الشرط مع امتناع عدم الموجب الواجب امتنع عدم مشروطه أيضاً. وهكذا إلى القديم الذي كلامنا فيه وهو المطلوب. وأما بطلان اللازم فبالاتفاق والدليل أما الاتفاق فلأن الأجسام عند الحكماء منحصرة في الفلكيات وحركاتها واجبة عندهم وفي العنصريات وحركاتها جائزة فلا شيء من الأجسام يمتنع عليه الحركة وأما الدليل فلان الأجسام متساوية في الماهية لتركبها من الجواهر الفردة المتماثلة كما عرفت فيصح على كل من الأجسام من الحيز ما صح على الآخر وما ذلك إلا بخروجه عن حيزه أو نقول : الأجسام إما بسيطة ويجوز على كل جزء منه أي من البسيط ما يصح على الآخر فيصح أن يماس بيساره ما يماسه بيمينه وبالعكس، وما هو إلا بالحركة وإما مركبة من البسائط فيصح على بسائطها من يماسها الآخر وما هو إلا بالحركة وبالجملة فنعلم بالضرورة أن مقولة الوضع غير واجبة للبسائط لأن أجزاءها متحدة في الماهية فيجوز تبدل أوضاعها نظرا إلى طبيعتها وكذا للمركبات لأن تبدل أوضاع البسائط التي فيها يستلزم تبدل أوضاعها و نعلم أيضاً بالضرورة أنه ما من جسم إلا ويمكن للقادر المختار الذي خلقه أن يغير وضعه فيجعل يمينه يساره وبالعكس، وإنكاره مكابرة لا يعتد بها
The Second Observatory - The First Objective: Bodies are created in four categories. It would be created, otherwise the eternal conditioned by it would be more deserving of creation, but rather that condition would also be eternal, and the discussion returns to it and to its emanation from the Necessary: is it with a condition or without a condition? And it is necessary to end at what must emanate from the Necessary without a condition to avoid infinite regress in things that are ordered and exist together. So if this final emanation ceased to exist, the Necessary would cease to exist — this is a contradiction. So if the non-existence of this condition is impossible along with the impossibility of the non-existence of the necessitating necessary, then the non-existence of its conditioned is also impossible. And so on until the eternal which is our subject, and that is what is sought. As for the invalidity of the necessary consequence, it is by consensus and proof. As for the consensus: because bodies according to the philosophers are confined to celestial bodies — their motions are necessary according to them — and to elemental bodies — their motions are contingent — so no body has motion impossible for it. As for the proof: because bodies are equal in essence due to their composition from similar indivisible atoms as you know, so whatever is possible for one body regarding position is possible for the other, and that is only by leaving its position. Or we say: bodies are either simple, and it is permissible for each part of it — i.e., of the simple — what is permissible for the other, so it is permissible for it to touch with its left what it touches with its right and vice versa, and that is only by motion; or composite from simples, and it is permissible for their simples what is permissible for the other, and that is only by motion. In short, we know necessarily that the category of position is not necessary for simples because their parts are identical in essence, so it is permissible for their positions to change considering their nature, and likewise for composites because the change of positions of the simples in them necessitates the change of their positions. And we also know necessarily that there is no body except that the powerful chooser who created it can change its position, making its right its left and vice versa, and its denial is stubbornness not to be considered.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof for the temporality of bodies and then refutes a potential objection. The author begins by stating the heading for the second observatory and first objective, which is that bodies are divided into four categories regarding creation. The proof itself starts with a conditional: if the body were not created (i.e., eternal), then the eternal conditioned by it would be more deserving of creation, leading to an infinite regress of conditions. The argument forces an end to a condition that must emanate from the Necessary Being without any prior condition, otherwise a contradiction arises: if that final conditioned entity ceased, the Necessary would cease. Since that is impossible, the conditioned must also be impossible to cease, tracing back to the eternal body in question, thus proving it must be created. The author then addresses the objection that the necessary consequence of this proof is false. He argues its invalidity by consensus and proof. The consensus is that philosophers divide bodies into celestial (with necessary motions) and elemental (with contingent motions), so no body has motion impossible for it, contradicting the proof's implication. The proof itself argues that all bodies are equal in essence, composed of similar atoms, so what is possible for one position-wise is possible for another, implying the ability to change position (motion). This applies to simples and composites alike. Additionally, we know necessarily that any body can have its position changed by the powerful chooser, so the necessary consequence is false, and the original proof stands.

Key Terms
حادث
قديم
جسم
كون
لازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The first proof relies on the idea that an eternal body cannot move.
Correction: The proof actually relies on the impossibility of an infinite regress of conditions, not on the impossibility of motion for an eternal body. Motion is addressed separately in the refutation.

Checkpoint
What is the key logical move in the first proof to avoid infinite regress?
A) Postulating that all bodies are composed of atoms.
B) Requiring an endpoint that emanates from the Necessary without condition.
C) Assuming that motion is impossible for eternal bodies.
D) Using consensus among philosophers.

Key Takeaway
The first proof shows bodies must be created because an eternal body would entail an infinite regress of conditions, which is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every aspect of your existence depends on conditions that ultimately trace back to the Necessary Being, where in your life have you been acting as if you are self-sufficient and independent of Allah? What will you change today to acknowledge your dependence?
?
The proof shows that an infinite chain of conditions is impossible. Are there created things—people, possessions, status—that you have elevated in your heart as if they were eternal and independent, forgetting they themselves are conditioned and dependent? How will you recalibrate your reliance?
?
This proof forces you to confront that you are not self-existent. What does that mean for how you view your own existence and purpose? How will you remind yourself daily of your utter dependence on the Necessary Being in your worship and thoughts?

Refutation of the Necessary Consequence: Bodies Can Move
Sanusi refutes the objection that the first proof's necessary consequence (eternal bodies cannot move) is false.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Sanusi refutes the objection that the first proof implies an eternal body cannot move, using consensus and a proof from the nature of bodies.

Core Idea
Bodies are equal in essence and can change position, so an eternal body could move, invalidating the objection.

After presenting the first proof for the temporality of bodies, the author addresses a potential objection: that the proof implies an eternal body would be impossible because it would require an eternal condition that cannot change. He refutes this by showing that bodies, by their nature, are capable of positional change, and this is supported by consensus and rational proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation
Read from 'وأما بطلان اللازم' to the end. Notice the two parts: consensus and proof.
2
Understand the consensus argument
The consensus claim is that philosophers divide bodies into celestial (with necessary motions) and elemental (with contingent motions), so no body is absolutely motionless.
This counters the supposed impossibility of motion for an eternal body.
3
Analyze the proof from essence
The proof argues that bodies are equal in essence (composed of similar atoms), so what is possible for one is possible for another regarding position. Since we observe bodies changing position, all bodies can change position, even if eternal.
The author also gives an alternative argument based on simples and composites.
4
Reflect on the final point
The author asserts we know necessarily that a powerful chooser can change any body's position, making denial mere stubbornness.
Thus the objection fails.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation, why does the consensus among philosophers show that the necessary consequence is false?
A) Because all bodies are composed of atoms.
B) Because celestial bodies have necessary motions and elemental bodies have contingent motions, so no body is motionless.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider something in your life that you have considered 'fixed' or unchangeable—whether a sin, a broken relationship, or a despairing heart. The passage says that denying the possibility of change is mere stubbornness (mukābara). Do you truly believe that the powerful chooser (al-qādir al-mukhtār) who created the heavens can change that situation? What would it take for you to stop insisting it's impossible and instead turn to Him in hope?
?
The author argues that bodies can change their position because the Creator can change their positions. What does your own resistance to change reveal about your trust in God's power? In what area of your life are you acting as if God's power is limited, and how can you actively submit to His ability to transform it?
?
The proof relies on the equality of bodies in essence: what is possible for one is possible for another. So if God can change the state of a person in the past (like from disbelief to faith), He can also change you. Has your awareness of God's absolute power ever genuinely moved you to repentance? If not, what is holding you back?
