The Second Proof: Avoiding Complications of the First
The author presents a dilemma-based proof for the temporality of bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the second proof for the temporality of bodies avoids the complications of the first by focusing on the accident of location (kawm) rather than motion and rest.

Core Idea
The second proof simplifies the argument by using an undisputed accident: location.

The first proof for the temporality of bodies required extensive discussions about the nature of motion and rest and whether rest is an existent accident. The second proof, attributed to later scholars, cuts through these complications by focusing on the accident of being in a place (kawm), which is uncontroversially existent. This lesson presents that argument, showing how it confronts the proponent of an eternal body with a decisive dilemma.

Guided Path
1
Understand the dilemma
The argument sets up a dilemma for anyone claiming an eternal body: either the body has an eternal accident of location, or it has an infinite series of such accidents. Both are shown to be impossible.
This is the core logical structure of the proof.
2
Examine the first horn: eternal kawm
If the body has a kawm not preceded by another, that kawm would have to be eternal, which is impossible as argued elsewhere.
3
Examine the second horn: infinite regress
If every kawm is preceded by another, an infinite regress of kawms arises, which is also impossible via standard arguments like tatbiq and tada'uf.
4
Appreciate the advantages over the first proof
The second proof avoids debates about rest being existent and about the possibility of a body being neither moving nor at rest in eternity.
This shows the elegance of the later formulation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسلك الثاني وهو لبعض المتأخرين كالاختصار للمسلك الأول أنه لو وجد جسم قديم لزم إما كون واحد قديم وإما أن يكون قبل كل كون كون آخر لا إلى نهاية والتالي باطل بقسميه أما الملازمة فلأنه لا بد للجسم من كون في حيز لكونه متحيزاً بالذات فإن وجد له كون غير مسبوق بآخر أي بكون آخر لزم القسم الأول لأن ذلك الكون يجب أن يكون ثابتاً للجسم القديم على الاستمرار فيكون قديماً وإلا أي وإن لم يوجد له كون غير مسبوق بآخر لزم القسم الثاني لأن كل كون له فإنه مسبوق يكون آخر فوجب أن يكون قبل كل كون كون لا إلى نهاية إذ على ذلك التقدير الذي نحن فيه لو وجد كون لا كون قبله لزم خلو الجسم عن الكون وأنت خبير بأن القسم الثاني لا يحتاج إلى هذا البيان لأنه إذا لم يوجد له كون غير مسبوق بآخر كان كل كون له مسبوقاً بكون قبله لا إلى نهاية إنما المحتاج إلى البيان هو القسم الأول، بأن يقال : ذلك الكون الذي ليس مسبوقاً أن يكون مستمراً أزلاً وإلا لزم خلو الجسم عن الكون، نعم لو قيل : إن وجد له كون قديم فهو القسم الأول، وإلا فلا بد أن يكون قبل كل كون كون آخر إذ لو وجد له كون لا كون قبله لزم خلو الجسم عن الكون لانتظم الكلام . وأما بطلان التالي فأما القسم الأول وهو قدم الكون فبمثل ما بينا به حدوث السكون، وأما القسم الثاني وهو تعاقب الأكوان إلى ما لا نهاية له فبالتطبيق وطريقة التضايف وغيرهما من أدلة بطلان التسلسل . ولا يخفى عليك أن في هذا المسلك طرحاً لمؤونات كثيرة كانت في المسلك الأول من بيان كون السكون وجودياً إذ قد اختلف فيه فذهب الحكماء إلى أنه عدم الحركة عما من شأنه الحركة فيجوز حينئذ زواله لأن إعدام الحوادث تزول بوجوداتها مع كونها أزلية فإن الكون الذي ذكر في هذا المسلك لا شك في أنه وجودي بل خلاف ومن بيان أن الجسم لا يخلو عن الحركة والسكون فإن لقائل أن يقول : هو في الأزل لا متحرك ولا ساكن لأن كلاً منهما يقتضي المسبوقية بالغير فلا يصح اتصافه بشيء منهما في الأزل ومن سقوط قولهم : السابقية والمسبوقية في الحركة بالفرض إذ لا أجزاء لها إلا بالوهم وفي الخارج هو أي الحركة كون واحد مستمر بين المبدأ والمنتهى لما مر من أن الحركة تطلق على الأمر الممتد ولا وجود له في الخارج بل يمتنع وجوده فيه وعلى الأمر المستمر الموجود الذي لا انقسام له في مأخذ الحركة وهو الذي يدعي أنه قديم لا المعنى الأول، فتأمل
The second way – it is by some later scholars as a summary of the first way: that if an eternal body existed, it would necessitate either a single eternal kawm (accident of being in a place) or that before every kawm there is another kawm ad infinitum. The consequent is false in both divisions. As for the implication: because a body necessarily has a kawm in a place due to being essentially located. If it has a kawm not preceded by another, i.e., by another kawm, the first division follows because that kawm must be permanently attached to the eternal body and thus be eternal. Otherwise, i.e., if it does not have a kawm not preceded by another, the second division follows because every kawm it has is preceded by another kawm, so there must be before every kawm a kawm ad infinitum. For on that hypothesis under discussion, if there were a kawm with no kawm before it, the body would be devoid of a kawm. And you know that the second division does not need this explanation because if it does not have a kawm not preceded by another, then every kawm it has is preceded by a kawm before it ad infinitum. Only the first division needs explanation, by saying: that kawm which is not preceded must be perpetually eternal, otherwise the body would be devoid of a kawm. Indeed, if it is said: if it has an eternal kawm, that is the first division; otherwise, there must be before every kawm another kawm, because if it had a kawm with no kawm before it, the body would be devoid of a kawm, for the argument to be coherent. As for the invalidity of the consequent: as for the first division, i.e., the eternity of the kawm, it is by the same method we proved the temporality of rest. As for the second division, i.e., the succession of kawms ad infinitum, it is by the method of application (tatbiq) and the method of mutual relation (tada'uf) and other proofs of the impossibility of infinite regress. And it is not hidden from you that this way discards many burdens that were in the first way: namely, the demonstration that rest is an existent (wujudi) – for there is disagreement about it, the philosophers holding that it is the absence of motion in that which is capable of motion, so it is permissible for it to cease because the non-existences of accidents cease with their existences despite being eternal – whereas the kawm mentioned in this way is undoubtedly existent, indeed it is the opposite; and the demonstration that a body is never devoid of motion and rest – for someone might say: in eternity it is neither moving nor at rest because each of them requires being preceded by another, so it cannot be described by either in eternity; and the collapse of their saying: priority and posteriority in motion are by assumption, because it has no parts except in estimation, while in external reality it – i.e., motion – is a single continuous kawm between the beginning and the end, because it has passed that motion is applied to the extended thing which has no existence externally, rather its existence is impossible there, and to the continuous existent thing which has no division in the locus of motion, and it is that which they claim is eternal, not the first meaning. So reflect.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument for the temporality of bodies, which is a summary of the first but avoids certain complications. The argument begins by assuming an eternal body. It then claims that such a body must have a 'kawm' (the accident of being in a place) because it is essentially located. Two possibilities arise: either the body has a kawm that is not preceded by another (making that kawm eternal, which is impossible), or every kawm is preceded by another, leading to an infinite regress of kawms. Both are shown to be false. The first division is refuted by the same method used to prove the temporality of rest, and the second by standard arguments against infinite regress (tatbiq and tada'uf). The author then notes the advantages of this argument over the first: it avoids the need to prove that rest is an existent accident (since kawm is undoubtedly existent), it avoids the objection that in eternity a body might be neither moving nor at rest, and it sidesteps the debate over motion being a continuous existent vs. an extended mental construct. The passage ends with an encouragement to reflect.

Misconception: The second proof is entirely different from the first proof.
Correction: It is a summary of the first proof, avoiding its complications but sharing the same underlying logic.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The second proof simplifies the argument for the temporality of bodies by using the accident of location, which is uncontroversially existent, thus avoiding the complexities of motion and rest.

The author ends with 'So reflect.' Have you honestly reflected, or are you merely collecting arguments? What specific change in your worldview or daily life will result from understanding this proof?
?
This proof simplifies complex issues by focusing on location. Are there areas in your faith where you seek complexity to avoid the simple, demanding truth? What is one simple truth you have been avoiding?

Al-Razi's Argument from Motion or Rest in Eternity
Al-Razi's argument from motion or rest in eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam al-Razi's argument that an eternal body would be either moving or at rest in eternity, both of which are impossible, thus proving the body must be created.

Core Idea
An eternal body cannot be moving or at rest—so it must be created.

Imam al-Razi presents a streamlined version of the first proof for the temporality of bodies. Instead of multiple premises, he focuses on a simple dichotomy: an eternal body would have to be either moving or at rest in eternity. Both options lead to contradictions. This lesson unpacks that concise argument and shows why it reinforces the Ash'arite position.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the author's note that this proof is derived from the first one, with fewer burdens.
The passage is brief; let the argument's logic sink in.
2
Identify the core dichotomy
The argument states that an eternal body must be either moving (متحرك) or at rest (ساكن) in eternity. Identify these two states and consider why each is impossible.
This is the key structure of the proof.
3
Understand the impossibility of both
If moving in eternity, change would occur in an eternal being, which is impossible. If at rest, that would imply permanence requiring time, also impossible. Both are false, so the body cannot be eternal.
The commentary explains this in more detail.
4
Reflect on the source of the proof
Note that al-Razi himself attributed this to earlier sources, and the author mentions it is taken from the first proof. Consider why this version is simpler.
This shows the interconnectedness of kalam arguments.
5
Complete the checkpoint
Answer the multiple-choice question to test your understanding of the argument's core dichotomy.
The answer is B: either moving or at rest.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسلك الثالث للإمام الرازي : ذكره في المحصل ونسبه الآمدي إلى بعض المتأخرين من الأشاعرة وهو أيضاً مأخوذ من المسلك الأول والمؤونات التي كانت فيه باقية هاهنا بحالها سوى قليل منها كما لا يخفى وتقريره أنه لو وجد جسم قديم لكان في الأزل إما متحركاً أو ساكناً والتالي باطل بقسميه وأنت بمعرفة بيانه بعد ما قررناه في المسلكين السابقين خبير فلا نشتغل حذفاً للمؤونة
The third way of Imam al-Razi: He mentioned it in al-Muhassal, and al-Amidi attributed it to some later Ash'arites. It is also taken from the first way, and the burdens that were in it remain here as they were except for a few, as is not hidden. Its formulation is that if an eternal body existed, it would be in eternity either moving or at rest, and the consequent is false in both divisions. And you, being knowledgeable of its explanation after what we established in the two previous ways, are expert, so we do not engage in it to save effort.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof for the temporality of bodies, attributed to Imam al-Razi and mentioned in his book al-Muhassal, also attributed by al-Amidi to later Ash'arites. The argument is derived from the first proof, but with fewer complexities. It states that if a body were eternal, then in eternity it would have to be either moving or at rest. Both options are impossible: if moving, it would imply change in an eternal being; if at rest, it would imply permanence that requires time. Since both are false, the body must be created. The author notes that the explanation is similar to the previous two proofs and thus does not elaborate further to save effort.

?

The argument refrains from elaboration to 'save effort'—yet you have the opportunity to reflect. In what area of your faith have you been relying on shortcuts instead of deep understanding? Commit to studying one proof of tawheed thoroughly this week.

The First Two Proofs for the Origination of Bodies
The author enumerates two possibilities for bodies and refutes each to prove origination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the two main proofs in kalām establish that bodies are originated (ḥādith) by refuting the possibilities of a beginningless attribute or an infinite regress.

Core Idea
The body must have a beginning: an eternal attribute or infinite regress is impossible.

The kalām theologians devised multiple proofs for the origination of bodies. This passage presents two concise proofs that avoid earlier complications. By examining the two possibilities—either a body possesses a beginningless attribute or an endless chain of attributes—the argument demonstrates that neither can hold, forcing the conclusion that bodies are originated.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get the overall argument. Note the two divisions presented at the beginning.
The author uses the term 'kawn' for attribute, which is a key term.
2
Identify the two possibilities
The passage divides bodies into two cases: either it has a beginningless attribute (first division) or it must have an infinite regress of attributes (second division). Understand why these are exhaustive.
The first case would mean the body contains an eternal accident, which is impossible.
3
Understand the refutations
For the first division, the refutation is similar to the proof against eternal rest. For the second, it uses arguments against infinite regress (taṭbīq and taḍāyuf). Read the commentary to see how each is refuted.
These refutations rely on earlier established principles.
4
Reflect on the advantages
The author notes that this new pair of proofs avoids many problems of the earlier 'first path', such as proving that rest is existent or that the body must always be in motion or rest. Consider why this is an improvement.
This shows the development of kalām argumentation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجد له كون قديم فهو القسم الأول، وإلا فلا بد أن يكون قبل كل كون كون آخر إذ لو وجد له كون لا كون قبله لزم خلو الجسم عن الكون لانتظم الكلام. وأما بطلان التالي فأما القسم الأول وهو قدم الكون فبمثل ما بينا به حدوث السكون، وأما القسم الثاني وهو تعاقب الأكوان إلى ما لا نهاية له فبالتطبيق وطريقة التضايف وغيرهما من أدلة بطلان التسلسل. ولا يخفى عليك أن في هذا المسلك طرحاً لمؤونات كثيرة كانت في المسلك الأول من بيان كون السكون وجودياً إذ قد اختلف فيه فذهب الحكماء إلى أنه عدم الحركة عما من شأنه الحركة فيجوز حينئذ زواله لأن إعدام الحوادث تزول بوجوداتها مع كونها أزلية فإن الكون الذي ذكر في هذا المسلك لا شك في أنه وجودي بل خلاف ومن بيان أن الجسم لا يخلو عن الحركة والسكون فإن لقائل أن يقول: هو في الأزل لا متحرك ولا ساكن لأن كلاً منهما يقتضي المسبوقية بالغير فلا يصح اتصافه بشيء منهما في الأزل ومن سقوط قولهم: السابقية والمسبوقية في الحركة بالفرض إذ لا أجزاء لها إلا بالوهم وفي الخارج هو أي الحركة كون واحد مستمر بين المبدأ والمنتهى لما مر من أن الحركة تطلق على الأمر الممتد ولا وجود له في الخارج بل يمتنع وجوده فيه وعلى الأمر المستمر الموجود الذي لا انقسام له في مأخذ الحركة وهو الذي يدعي أنه قديم لا المعنى الأول، فتأمل
If a body has a beginningless attribute (kawn qadīm), then it is the first division; otherwise it must be that before every attribute there is another attribute, for if it had an attribute with no attribute before it, the body would be devoid of the attribute, as the statement is arranged. And as for the refutation of the consequent: as for the first division, which is the eternity of the attribute, it is by the same kind as we demonstrated the origination of rest (sukūn); and as for the second division, which is the succession of attributes indefinitely, it is by the method of correlation (taṭbīq) and the method of mutual relation (taḍāyuf) and other proofs of the invalidity of the infinite regress (tasalsul). And it is not hidden from you that in this path there is a casting aside of many burdens that were in the first path, such as the demonstration that rest (sukūn) is existent—for there is disagreement about it, the philosophers (al-ḥukamā') holding that it is the absence of motion from what is capable of motion, so its disappearance is permissible at that time because the non-existence of accidents is removed by their existences while they are eternal—for the attribute (kawn) mentioned in this path is undoubtedly existent, rather it is different; and such as the demonstration that the body is never free of motion and rest, for one might say: in pre-eternity it is neither moving nor at rest because each of them requires being preceded by another, so it is not correct to be described by either of them in pre-eternity; and such as the falling away of their statement: priority and being preceded in motion are by supposition, for it has no parts except in the imagination (wahm), and in the external world it, i.e. motion, is a single continuous attribute between the beginning and the end, because it has been mentioned that motion is applied to the extended thing, which does not exist in the external world, rather its existence is impossible in it, and to the continuous existent thing which has no division in the source of motion, and it is what they claim is eternal, not the first meaning. So reflect.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage presents the first two proofs (maslakān) for the origination of bodies. The author begins by dividing bodies into two cases: either a body has a beginningless attribute (kawn qadīm) or it does not. If it does, that attribute is eternal, which is impossible because it would be an eternal accident. If it does not, then an infinite regress of attributes would be required, which is also impossible due to the arguments against regress (method of correlation and mutual relation). The author then notes that this pair of proofs avoids many difficulties of an earlier proof (the first path mentioned in a previous discussion). Those difficulties included the need to prove that rest is existent (since some philosophers considered it a mere absence of motion) and the need to argue that a body is never free of motion or rest. The author points out that in this new proof, the attribute (kawn) is clearly existent, and there is no need to discuss whether the body in pre-eternity is moving or at rest because both options require a predecessor. Furthermore, the notion of priority in motion is by supposition; in reality, motion is a single continuous attribute. Thus the two possibilities are refuted, establishing that bodies must have a beginning.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The first two proofs require that a body be empty of all attributes in pre-eternity.
Correction: The proofs only address the impossibility of a beginningless attribute or infinite regress; they do not claim the body is devoid of attributes. The attribute (kawn) is accepted as existent.

Checkpoint
What are the two possibilities considered for a body in the first two proofs?
A) Either it has a beginningless attribute or it has a succession of attributes indefinitely.
B) Either it is composed of atoms or it is composed of matter and form.
C) Either it is necessarily existent or it is possible.
D) Either it is at rest or it is in motion in pre-eternity.

Key Takeaway
The two proofs show that a body cannot have a beginningless attribute nor an infinite regress of attributes, so it must have a beginning and therefore be originated (ḥādith).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read a logical proof that your very existence is not eternal—you have a beginning. Does knowing this intellectually truly change the way you live today, or do you still act as if you will never cease to exist? What one habit would be different if you genuinely felt the reality of your contingency?
?
The author invites you to 'reflect' (tā'ammal). When was the last time you sat still and reflected on the signs of creation as indicators of the Creator, not through emotion alone but through careful reasoning? What stops you from doing that regularly?
?
This passage sets aside many burdens and simplifies the proof. In your spiritual journey, are you willing to let go of unnecessary complexities and hold firmly to clear, rational proofs of your dependence on Allah? What complexities are you holding onto that keep you from true submission?

The Sixth Path: Bodies as Loci for Accidents
Defines the sixth path and its empirical basis for proving bodily origination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the sixth path proves the origination of bodies by establishing that bodies are loci for originated accidents, and why this path is considered in the same ruling as the first two.

Core Idea
Bodies are originated because they host accidents, and the eternal cannot host accidents.

The author now presents the sixth path for demonstrating the origination of bodies. This path builds on the observation that bodies undergo changes—movements, colors, shapes—which are originated events. Since the eternal cannot be subject to such changes, bodies must be originated. The author also clarifies that this path is essentially equivalent to the first two, avoiding unnecessary multiplication of arguments.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
The passage begins by noting that the previous paths only prove origination of bodies and their attributes, and require negation of incorporeal entities to generalize. The author then explains why he didn't include the third path and clarifies that the sixth is like the first two.
This sets up that the sixth path is not a new independent proof but reinforces the same conclusion.
2
Read the definition of the sixth path
Read the sentence 'المسلك السادس: الجسم يقوم به الحادث' and its translation. Reflect on what it means for a body to be that to which the originated accident subsists.
The term 'يقوم به' indicates that accidents inhere in the body.
3
Observe the empirical evidence
The author provides observational examples: movements, lights, colors, and shapes are originated and we see them in bodies. This empirical evidence grounds the proof.
This shows that the proof relies on direct experience.
4
Connect to the nature of the eternal
The passage concludes by stating that nothing eternal can be a locus for originated things, which will be proven later. Thus, bodies cannot be eternal because they host originated accidents.
This establishes the logical conclusion that bodies are originated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كذلك فإنهما لا يعطيان إلا حدوث الأجسام وصفاتها ويحتاج في تعميمها إلى نفي المجردات ولم يتعرض للمسلك الثالث لأنه جعله عين الأول لبقاء المؤونات وأما السادس فهو في حكم الأولين بلا اشتباه. المسلك السادس: الجسم يقوم به الحادث وهو ضروري لما نشاهده من حدوث الحركات القائمة به وتجدد الأعراض الحالة فيه كالأضواء والألوان والأشكال وغيرها، ولا شيء من القديم لما سنبرهن عليه في الإلهيات من أن القديم لا يكون محلا للحوادث.
Likewise, they only give the origination of bodies and their attributes, and in generalizing them one needs to negate incorporeal entities. He did not address the third path because he considered it identical to the first due to remaining difficulties. As for the sixth, it is in the same ruling as the first two without doubt. The sixth path: The body is that to which the originated [accident] subsists, and it is necessary due to what we observe of the origination of movements subsisting in it and the renewal of accidents occurring in it such as lights, colors, shapes, and others. And nothing of the eternal [can be so] because we will prove in the divine matters that the eternal cannot be a locus for originated things.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage defines the sixth path (المسلك السادس) for proving the origination of bodies. The author first notes that the previous paths only prove the origination of bodies and their attributes, and to generalize they need to negate incorporeal substances. He then clarifies that the sixth path is essentially the same ruling as the first two. The sixth path itself states that a body is that to which the originated accident subsists; this is necessary because we observe originated movements and accidents like lights, colors, shapes. The eternal cannot have such accidents, as will be proven in the section on divine attributes. This establishes that bodies are originated because they are loci for originated accidents, and the eternal cannot be such a locus.

Key Terms
المسلك السادس
الحادث
القديم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The sixth path is a completely separate and independent proof from the first two.
Correction: The author explicitly states that the sixth path is 'in the same ruling as the first two without doubt', meaning it is essentially equivalent and not a new independent argument.

Checkpoint
What is the key empirical observation that supports the sixth path?
A) Bodies are composed of atoms.
B) Bodies undergo movement and change of accidents like colors and shapes.
C) Bodies are eternal.
D) Bodies are incorporeal.

Key Takeaway
The sixth path proves bodies are originated because they are observed to be the locus for originated accidents, and the eternal cannot be such a locus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your body is constantly hosting originated accidents — movements, colors, even your breath. If nothing eternal can host such things, what does that force you to conclude about your own origin and your total dependence on the Creator? Are you living with that awareness every moment?
?
You just learned that the eternal cannot be a locus for originated things. Look at your own life: you are a locus for countless originated changes — thoughts, emotions, physical states. How does that undeniable fact confront you with the reality that you are not self-sufficient? What would it look like to live today in conscious dependence on the One who alone is eternal?
?
If every accident in your body — every heartbeat, every thought — is originated and passing, and you are the locus of those accidents, then you are proof of your own origination. What is stopping you from fully submitting to the Eternal as your Lord and Originator? What would you need to let go of to embrace that?

Refuting the Material Cause Argument for the Eternity of Bodies
Presents and refutes the material cause argument for the eternity of bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponent's material cause argument for the eternity of bodies and the systematic refutation presented by the theologian.

Core Idea
Matter is not eternal; the composition of body from matter and form is denied.

The opponents of Islamic theology often employed arguments based on Greek philosophy to claim the eternity of the universe. One such argument relies on the concept of matter as a material cause. In this passage, the theologian presents the objection and then dismantles it through multiple lines of attack, rejecting the very premises on which it rests.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the structure: first the objection, then the answer with several points.
2
Identify the three refutation points
The answer contains three distinct refutations: denial of composition, denial of matter's eternity, and denial that matter always has form. Find each one in the text.
The first point is the strongest: it questions the very framework of matter and form.
3
Understand the logic behind the refutation
Ask yourself why the theologian denies that matter is eternal even if composition is granted. The argument mentions varying predispositions and essential necessity, which will be refuted later.
This shows the interconnectedness of theological arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتج الخصم على القدم بشبه أربع: الأولى وهي مستخرجة من العلة المادية أن يقال: المادة قديمة وإلا احتاجت إلى مادة أخرى، لما عرفت من أن كل حادث مسبوق بالمادة، وتسلسل أي لزم التسلسل في المواد، وإنها أي المادة لا تخلو عن الصورة الجسمية والنوعية أيضا لما تقدم، فيلزم قدم الجسم لكون أجزائه بأسرها قديمة. والجواب: منع تركب الجسم من المادة والصورة، وإن سلمنا ذلك لا نسلم كون المادة قديمة، فإنه أي كونها قديمة يثبت بوجود اختلاف الاستعداد المقرب إلى وجود الحوادث كما سلف، وإنه فرع الإيجاب بالذات وسنبطله بإثبات قدرة الصانع في الموقف الخامس، ولا نسلم أيضا أنها لا تخلو عن الصورة وقد مر ضعف دليله.
The opponent argued for eternity with four [sophistries]: The first, taken from the material cause, is that matter is eternal; otherwise it would need another matter, since every originated thing is preceded by matter, leading to an infinite regress. And matter is never devoid of corporeal and specific form, as previously established, so the body is eternal because all its parts are eternal. The answer: We deny that the body is composed of matter and form. Even if we grant that, we do not concede that matter is eternal, for its eternity is supposedly proven by the existence of varying predispositions bringing about originated things, as previously mentioned, and that is a consequence of essential necessity, which we will disprove by proving the power of the Creator in the fifth station. Also, we do not concede that matter is never devoid of form; the weakness of its proof has already been shown.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection (شبهة الأولى) from the material cause. The opponent argues that matter must be eternal because if it were originated, it would need prior matter, leading to an infinite regress. Also, matter always has form, so the body's parts are eternal, making the body eternal. The answer has several points: (1) Denial that body is composed of matter and form (i.e., the Aristotelian composition is not accepted). (2) Even if we accept composition, we deny that matter is eternal; rather, its supposed eternity is based on varying predispositions (استعداد) which is a consequence of essential necessity (الإيجاب بالذات), which will be refuted later. (3) We also deny that matter is never devoid of form; the argument for that already was shown weak.

Key Terms
العلة المادية
المادة
الصورة
الاستعداد
الإيجاب بالذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Matter is eternal because otherwise an infinite regress of matters would occur.
Correction: The theologian denies the premises: bodies are not composed of matter and form in the Aristotelian sense, and even if they were, matter is not eternal; its supposed eternity is based on unproven assumptions.

Checkpoint
What is the first refutation point against the material cause argument?
A) The body is composed of matter and form.
B) We deny that the body is composed of matter and form.
C) Matter is originated.
D) The body is eternal.

Key Takeaway
The material cause argument fails because its foundational premises—that bodies are composed of matter and form, that matter is eternal, and that matter always has form—are all denied or shown to be unproven.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about the origins of the universe, do you unknowingly adopt assumptions from philosophical systems like Aristotle's? What would it cost you to abandon those assumptions if they contradicted revelation?
?
The author denies that bodies are composed of matter and form—a seemingly obvious truth. Are you holding onto any 'obvious' beliefs about the world that you have never critically examined? How would you feel if they were dismantled?
?
This refutation will only be complete when divine power is proven. Are you intellectually restless enough to demand that God's power, not human philosophy, be the ultimate explanation? Or do you prefer conclusions that leave you in control?

Second Objection from Time (Formal Cause) and Its Refutation
Presents and refutes the argument that time is eternal based on formal cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the refutation of the second objection from formal cause distinguishes temporal priority from essential priority to avoid contradiction.

Core Idea
Time's priority is essential, not temporal, so no contradiction arises.

This passage presents a powerful objection to the createdness of the world: if time is not eternal, then its non-existence before existence would involve a temporal priority that leads to a contradiction. The opponent argues that time must be eternal, implying the eternity of motion and body. The refutation skillfully disentangles two types of priority, showing that the priority of time's non-existence is essential, not temporal, thus defusing the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, carefully read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to grasp the overall objection and answer.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
Notice the opponent's argument: time must be eternal, otherwise temporal priority would cause a contradiction.
3
Analyze the structure
The passage divides into an objection (conditional reasoning) and a two-part answer (denial, then redefinition of priority).
4
Understand the refutation moves
First, temporal priority is denied because it presupposes time. Second, even if priority exists, it is essential, not temporal.
5
Connect to broader theology
Reflect on why this distinction matters for proving the world's createdness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثانية وقد نسبها الإمام الرازي إلى العلة الصورية أن يقال: الزمان قديم وإلا كان عدمه قبل وجوده قبلية لا يجامع فيها السابق المسبوق وهو السبق الزماني فيكون موجوداً حين ما فرض معدوماً، هذا خلف. وإذا كان الزمان قديماً كانت الحركة التي هو مقدارها قديماً فكذا الجسم الذي هو محل الحركة. والجواب: منع أن التقدم بالزمان، أي لا نسلم تحقق التقدم الزماني فإنه فرع وجود الزمان وهو غير مسلم، وإن سلم تحققه في الجملة فليس تقدم عدم الزمان على وجوده بالزمان حتى يلزم اجتماع النقيضين بل هو كتقدم أجزاء الزمان بعضها على بعض، أعني التقدم بالذات لا بأمر زائد عليها فلا محذور حينئذ.
The second sophistry, attributed by Imam al-Razi to the formal cause, is that time is eternal; otherwise its non-existence before its existence would be a priority that does not involve a preceding and preceded [relationship] — this is temporal priority, so it would be existent at the moment it was assumed non-existent, which is a contradiction. And if time is eternal, then motion, of which time is the measure, is eternal, and likewise the body which is the locus of motion. The answer: We deny temporal priority; we do not concede the reality of temporal priority, for it is a consequence of the existence of time, which is not granted. And even if its reality is granted in general, the priority of time's non-existence over its existence is not temporal, so as to entail the conjunction of opposites; rather, it is like the priority of some parts of time over others — that is, priority by essence, not by something additional, so there is no problem.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection (شبهة الثانية) from the formal cause, attributed to Fakhr al-Din al-Razi. The opponent argues that time must be eternal; otherwise, its non-existence before existence would involve a temporal priority that leads to a contradiction. If time is eternal, then motion (which time measures) is eternal, and the body (locus of motion) is eternal. The answer: We deny that there is a real temporal priority because that presupposes time itself. Even if we grant some kind of priority, it is not temporal priority but essential priority (تقدم بالذات), like the priority of one part of time over another — this does not lead to a contradiction. So the objection fails.

Key Terms
الزمان
التقدم بالزمان
التقدم بالذات
العلة الصورية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Time must be eternal because its non-existence before existence would involve a temporal priority that leads to contradiction.
Correction: The priority is essential (تقدم بالذات), not temporal, so no contradiction arises.

Checkpoint
How does the answer avoid the contradiction in the argument about time?
A) By denying the existence of time.
B) By asserting that time is originated.
C) By distinguishing temporal priority from essential priority.
D) By accepting the argument.

Key Takeaway
The refutation of the second objection shows that time's priority is essential, not temporal, thus preserving the possibility of time's createdness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, do you instinctively think of priority in terms of time? When you consider your own existence, do you ever reflect that your dependence on Allah is essential, not temporal—that He is prior to you in essence, not just in time? How would your sense of urgency in worship change if you truly felt that priority today?
?
The objection assumes that if time were created, its non-existence would have to be temporally prior—an assumption you might make about your own habits or beliefs. Can you identify a belief you hold that you think is justified because you've always held it (temporal priority), but perhaps you have never examined whether it is actually prior in essence? Will you test it today?
?
This passage redefines priority to avoid a contradiction. Are there areas in your life where you assume a certain framework (like time) is the only way to understand something, and that leads you to doubt Allah's power? What would it look like to adopt the framework of essential priority in your trust in Allah's decree?

Third Objection from Efficient Cause and the Answer by Counterexample of Daily Events
Presents the efficient cause argument and its refutation by counterexample.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the efficient cause argument for the world's eternity and its refutation through the counterexample of daily originated events.

Core Idea
The strongest objection to temporal creation is refuted by showing it would also make daily events eternal.

In this passage, the opponent presents what they consider their strongest argument—the efficient cause argument for the world's eternity. The author then offers a powerful refutation by using the very same reasoning to argue that if it were valid, it would also entail that everyday events like sunrise and sunset are eternal, which is obviously false. This lesson walks through both the objection and the counterexample.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and its reformulation
Start by reading the efficient cause argument in its original and simpler forms. Notice how the opponent tries to prove the world's eternity by analyzing God's agency.
The objection is considered the strongest of three.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
The objection forces a choice: either God's agency is eternal (so the world is eternal) or it is originated (leading to infinite regress). Both lead to the world's eternity.
3
Examine the first response: the counterexample
Read the author's first reliable answer: the counterexample of daily events. If the opponent's reasoning were sound, then every daily event would have to be eternal, which is absurd.
This is a classic refutation by showing the argument proves too much.
4
Follow the opponent's evasion and the author's rebuttal
The opponent tries to evade by claiming daily events involve an infinite regress of astronomical conditions. The author rebuts by stating that infinite regress is impossible whether simultaneous or successive, and also turns the tables by applying the same logic to the world's creation.
The passage cuts off during the second reply; only the first response is complete.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثالثة: وهي العمدة عندهم في إثبات مطلبهم ومأخوذة من العلة المؤثرة أن يقال: فاعلية الفاعل للعالم أي تأثيره فيه وإيجاده إياه قديمة ويلزم منه قدم العالم. بيانه أنه لو كانت فاعليته حادثة مخصوصة بوقت معين لتوقفت على شرط حادث مختص بذلك الوقت، وإلا أي وإن لم تتوقف على شرط كذلك لزم الترجيح بلا مرجح لأن اختصاص حدوث الفاعلية حينئذ بذلك الوقت دون ما قبله وما بعده مع تساوي نسبتها إلى جميع الأوقات تخصيص بلا مخصص، والكلام في ذلك الشرط الحادث واختصاصه بوقت معين كما في الحادث الأول فلا بد له أيضاً من شرط آخر حادث. ويلزم التسلسل في الشروط الحادثة. وإذا كانت فاعليته قديمة كان الأثر قديماً أيضاً إذ لا يتصور تحقق تأثير وإيجاد حقيقي في زمان مع عدم حصول الأثر فيه. وقد تقرر هذه الشبهة بعبارة أخرى أبسط: فيقال ما لا بد منه في الإيجاد إن كان حاصلاً أزلاً كان الإيجاد حاصلاً فيه إذ لو لم يحصل لكان حصوله بعده إما أن يتوقف على شرط حادث فلا يكون ما لا بد منه حاصلاً وهو خلاف المفروض، أو لا يتوقف فيلزم الترجيح بلا مرجح، وإذا كان الإيجاد أزلياً كان وجود الأثر الذي لا يتخلف عنه كذلك. وإن لم يكن جميع ما لا بد منه في الإيجاد حاصلاً في الأزل كان بعضه حادثاً قطعاً فنقل الكلام إليه ونقول: وإن لم يحتج هذا الحادث إلى إيجاد لزم استغناء الحادث عن المؤثر المخصص، وإن احتاج فإما أن يكون جميع ما لا بد منه في إيجاده حاصلا في الأزل فيلزم قدم الحادث، أو لا يكون حاصلاً فبعضه حادث بالضرورة فيلزم التسلسل في الأسباب والمسببات وهو محال. وقد ذكر في الجواب عنه وجوه والذي يصلح للتعويل عليه وجهان: الأول: النقض بالحادث اليومي إذ لا شبهة في وجوده فنقول: فاعلية الفاعل القديم لهذا الحادث قديمة إذ لو كانت حادثة لوقفت على شرط حادث حذراً من الترجيح بلا مرجح، والكلام في هذا الشرط الحادث كما في الأول فتتسلسل الحوادث المترتبة إلى ما لا نهاية له، فلو صح دليلكم لكان الحادث اليومي قديماً. لا يقال: إنه أي الحادث اليومي يستند إلى الحوادث الفلكية من الحركات والاتصالات الكوكبية وكل منها مسبوق بآخر لا إلى نهاية، ومثل هذا التسلسل جائز بخلاف التسلسل في الأمور المترتبة المجتمعة. لأنا نقول: ابتداء الفارق بين صورة النقض ومحل النزاع على الوجه الذي ذكرتموه لا يدفع النقض لأن التسلسل في الأمور التي ضبطها وجود سواء كانت مجتمعة أو متعاقبة محال كما وقفت عليه. وأيضاً فنقول: إذا سلم جواز التسلسل في الحوادث المتعاقبة فلم لا يجوز أن يكون حدوث العالم مشروطا بشرط مسبوق بآخر لا إلى نهاية فيكون حدوث العالم عن المبدأ القديم بتسلسل الحوادث المتعاقبة كما في الحادث اليومي عندكم. فإن قيل: ذلك أي تسلسل الشروط المتعاقبة إنما يتصور فيما له مادة بتزايد استعدادها بتوارد تلك الشروط عليها لقبول الحادث المشروط بتلك الشروط حتى إذا كمل الاستعداد فاض عليها من المبدأ القديم ما هي مستعدة له، وما سوى العالم أي ما هو خارج عنه ليس له مادة حتى يتصور توارد الشروط المعتبرة في حدوث العالم عليها. قلنا: لا نسلم ذلك الذي ذكرتموه من أن الشروط والحوادث المتعاقبة إنما يتصور في الماديات إذ قد تكون تصورات متعاقبة لأمر مجرد عن المادة وتوابعها كل سابق منها شرط للاحق إلى أن تنتهي فيما يزال إلى ما هو شرط أي إلى تصور هو شرط لحدوث
The third sophistry: it is the main one for them in proving their claim, taken from the efficient cause: that the agency of the agent for the world—i.e., His influencing and bringing it into existence—is eternal, and the eternity of the world follows from it. Its explanation: if His agency were originated, specific to a particular time, it would depend on an originated condition specific to that time; otherwise, if it did not depend on such a condition, then preference without a preferrer would follow, because the specification of the origination of agency at that time rather than before or after, given the equal relation to all times, is specification without a specifier. And the discussion about that originated condition and its specification to a particular time is as in the first originated thing, so it too must have another originated condition. Thus infinite regress in originated conditions follows. And if His agency is eternal, then the effect is also eternal, because a real influence and bringing into existence at a time cannot be conceived without the effect occurring in it. This sophistry has been presented in another, simpler wording: it is said that if that which is necessary for bringing into existence is present from eternity, then the bringing into existence is present in it, because if it were not, its occurrence afterwards would either depend on an originated condition—so that which is necessary would not be present, contrary to the assumption—or it would not depend, then preference without a preferrer would follow. And if the bringing into existence is eternal, then the existence of the effect, which does not lag behind it, is likewise. If not all that is necessary for bringing into existence is present from eternity, then some of it is necessarily originated, and the discussion shifts to it. We say: if this originated thing does not need to be brought into existence, then the originated thing would be independent of the specifying efficient cause; if it needs, then either all that is necessary for its bringing into existence is present from eternity, thus the originated thing would be eternal, or it is not present, so some of it is originated necessarily, leading to infinite regress in causes and effects, which is impossible. It has been mentioned in the answer several aspects, and what is reliable are two: the first: the counterexample by the daily originated event, for there is no doubt about its existence. We say: the agency of the eternal agent for this event is eternal, because if it were originated it would depend on an originated condition to avoid preference without a preferrer, and the discussion about this originated condition is as in the first, so an infinite series of ordered originated events would follow. If your argument were valid, the daily event would be eternal. It cannot be said that it—i.e., the daily event—depends on astronomical events such as motions and planetary conjunctions, each preceded by another without end, and this kind of regress is permissible, unlike regress in simultaneous ordered things. For we say: the mere distinction between the form of the counterexample and the subject of dispute in the way you mentioned does not refute the counterexample, because regress in things whose existence is fixed, whether simultaneous or successive, is impossible, as you have established. Also, we say: if the permissibility of regress in successive events is granted, why is it not allowed that the world’s origination be conditioned by a condition preceded by another without end, so that the world’s origination from the eternal principle occurs through a series of successive events, as in the case of the daily event according to you? For if it is said: that—i.e., the series of successive conditions—is only conceivable for something that has matter, whose predisposition increases through the succession of those conditions upon it to receive the event conditioned by those conditions, so that when the predisposition is complete, from the eternal principle flows what it is predisposed for; and what is other than the world—i.e., what is outside it—has no matter so that the succession of conditions considered in the world’s origination upon it could be conceived. We say: we do not concede what you mentioned, that conditions and successive events are only conceivable in material things, because there can be successive concepts for an immaterial thing devoid of matter and its accessories, each preceding being a condition for the subsequent until they end, in what continues, to that which is a condition—i.e., to a concept that is a condition for origination.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection (شبهة الثالثة), which is considered the strongest, based on the efficient cause. The opponent argues that God's agency must be either eternal or originated. If eternal, the world is eternal; if originated, it requires a further condition leading to infinite regress. The author then gives a simpler formulation. The answer offers two valid responses; only the first is fully presented here. The first response is the counterexample of daily events (النقض بالحادث اليومي): if the opponent's reasoning were sound, then every daily event would also be eternal, which is absurd. The opponent might try to evade by saying that daily events are caused by astronomical cycles with an infinite regress, but the author rejects this by stating that any infinite regress in things whose existence is fixed is impossible, whether simultaneous or successive. The author then turns the tables: if the opponent allows infinite regress in successive events for daily events, then the same could apply to the world's creation. The opponent then objects that such regress only applies to material things with predispositions, but the author replies that immaterial things can also have successive conceptual conditions. The passage cuts off at this point.

Key Terms
العلة المؤثرة
الفاعلية
الإيجاد
التسلسل
النقض بالحادث اليومي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The answer simply rejects infinite regress without further argument.
Correction: The answer actually uses the opponent's own acceptance of regress in daily events to undermine the argument; it does not simply deny regress.

Checkpoint
What is the first valid response to the third objection?
A) Denial of efficient causality.
B) The counterexample of daily originated events.
C) Affirmation of infinite regress.
D) Denial of the necessity of a condition.

Key Takeaway
The efficient cause argument fails because it would imply the eternity of everyday events, which is absurd.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author uses a simple daily event to dismantle a sophisticated philosophical argument. Think of a daily blessing you take for granted. Could it be that your very existence requires a similar refutation of the doubts you hold? Will you pause today to see God's agency in the ordinary?
?
If the opponent's argument were true, even your morning coffee would be eternal. How does the refutation of this argument shift your perspective on the moment-by-moment creation you experience? Will you live today with the awareness that each moment is new creation?

Bodies are Permanent: The Argument from Rational Necessity
Al-Iji presents the Ash'ari proof that bodies are permanent, responds to the objection from sensory illusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari proof that bodies are permanent, based on rational necessity, and how they respond to the objection from sensory illusion.

Core Idea
Rational necessity, not mere sensation, confirms that bodies persist through time.

In this passage, al-Iji presents the Ash'ari position that bodies are permanent (bāqiya), contrary to al-Nazzām's view that they are continuously renewed. The key proof is grounded in rational necessity (ḍarūra 'aqliyya), which we all intuitively recognize. An objection from sensory illusion is raised and decisively refuted.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim and opposition
Read the opening line: 'Bodies are permanent, contrary to al-Nazzām.' Understand that this is the thesis being defended.
Al-Nazzām held that bodies are renewed moment by moment like accidents.
2
Understand al-Amidi's argument from rational necessity
Examine al-Amidi's statement: we know by rational necessity that the mountains, earth, and heavens we saw yesterday are the same today. This is a self-evident truth.
Rational necessity means the mind immediately affirms this without proof.
3
Grasp the objection from sensory illusion
The opponent argues: your certainty is only due to sensory permanence, but sensation is unreliable—it also makes accidents appear permanent, yet you deny their permanence.
The objection challenges consistency in using sensory testimony.
4
Follow the Ash'ari response
The response denies that the certainty is merely sensory. Instead, it is rational necessity, which is self-evident and independent of sensation.
The self-evident (badīhī) does not require a basis; it is affirmed by innate disposition (fiṭra).
5
Consider the supporting evidences
If bodies were not permanent, death/life and transformations (e.g., heating, cooling) would be impossible because they require a single subject. This is absurd, so bodies must be permanent.
These are additional rational proofs that reinforce the claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الثالث : الأجسام باقية خلافاً للنظام ( الأجسام باقية خلافاً للنظام فإنه ذهب إلى أنها متجددة آنا فاناً كالأعراض. وقيل : هذا النقل عنه غير معتمد عليه لأنه قال باحتياج الأجسام إلى المؤثر حال البقاء فتوهمت النقلة أنه لا يقول ببقائها ( ومن أصحابنا ) أي ومن الأشاعرة (من ادعى فيه الضرورة أي ،البداهة قال الآمدي : نحن نعلم بالضرورة العقلية أن ما شاهدناه بالأمس من الجبال الراسيات والأرضين والسماوات هو عين ما نشاهده اليوم وكذا نعلم بالاضطرار أن من فاتحناه بالكلام هو عين من ختمناه معه. وإن أولادنا ورفقاءنا الآن الذين كانوا معنا من قبل ( لا يقال : ليس ذلك ) أي جزمنا ببقائها ضرورة ) إلا لبقائها في الحس) فإنه يشهد باستمرار الأجسام ( ولا يصلح) الحس وشهادته بالبقاء ( للتعويل عليه ) والوثوق به ( إذ الأعراض كذلك ) لأن الحسن شاهد ببقائها . ( وقد قلتم : ( أيها الأشاعرة ( بأنها لا تبقى ) زمانين بل هناك أمثال متجددة لم يدرك الحس تفاوتها فحسبها أمراً واحداً مستمراً فكيف تقبلون شهادته في الأجسام دون الأعراض ؟ ( قلنا : ( أي لأنا نقول : ( لا نسلم أن ذلك ) الجزم منا (ليس إلا للبقاء في الحس حتى يتجه عليه ما ذكرتموه ) بل الضرورة العقلية حاصلة ) بلا شبهة ( والضروري ) البديهي ( لا يطلب مستنده بل هو ما يجزم به مجرد الفطرة ) عند تصور الطرفين وملاحظة النسبة فإن ذلك هو معنى البديهي المرادف للأولى ( ومنهم من استدل عليه بأنه لو لم تكن الأجسام باقية لارتفع الموت والحياة ) أي لم يكن أن يقال لموت حي أو حياة ميت لأن محلهما يجب أن يكون واحداً وعلى ذلك التقدير فالجسم حال حياته غير الجسم حال مماته فلا يكونان واردين على موضوع واحد ( و ) لارتفع ( التسخن والتبرد والتسود والتبيض ونظائرها، أي لم يكن القول بالاستحالة أصلا بأنها مشروطة باتحاد المحل ) وكل ذلك باطل بالضرورة ) العقلية
The Third Inquiry: Bodies are permanent, contrary to al-Nazzam. Bodies are permanent, contrary to al-Nazzam, for he held that they are renewed moment by moment like accidents. And it is said: This report about him is not reliable because he said that bodies need an effecter in the state of permanence, so the transmitters assumed that he does not affirm their permanence. And among our companions (i.e., among the Ash'aris) there are those who claimed necessity (i.e., self-evidence) for it. Al-Amidi said: We know by rational necessity that what we saw yesterday of the firm mountains, the earths, and the heavens is identical to what we see today; likewise, we know by necessity that the one we initiated speech with is the same one we concluded with, and that our children and companions now are those who were with us before. It cannot be said: That (i.e., our certainty of their permanence) is only due to their permanence in sensation, for sensation testifies to the continuity of bodies, and sensation and its testimony to permanence are not reliable, because accidents are also such (i.e., sensation testifies to their permanence). And you (Ash'aris) have said that they do not remain for two moments, but rather there are successive similars whose difference sensation does not perceive, so it deems them a single continuous thing; so how do you accept its testimony for bodies but not for accidents? We reply: We do not grant that this certainty of ours is only due to permanence in sensation, such that what you mentioned would apply; rather, rational necessity is present without doubt. And the necessary (i.e., self-evident) does not seek its basis; rather, it is what the mere innate disposition (fitra) affirms upon conceiving the two extremes and observing the relation—that is the meaning of self-evident, synonymous with primary. And some of them adduced evidence for it: if bodies were not permanent, then death and life would be impossible—i.e., one could not say 'death of a living being' or 'life of a dead being' because their locus must be one, but on this assumption the body during life is other than the body during death, so they would not be incident on a single subject; and also heating, cooling, blackening, whitening, and the like would be impossible—i.e., the very concept of transformation would not exist because it is conditioned by unity of locus. And all of this is void by rational necessity.
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage presents the Ash'ari position that bodies are permanent, contrary to al-Nazzam who held that bodies are renewed moment by moment. It begins by noting that some doubt the report that al-Nazzam denied permanence, but the main argument follows. The author (al-Iji or al-Jurjani in the Sharh) quotes al-Amidi: we know by rational necessity that the same mountains, earth, and heavens persist—our certainty is not merely sensory. An objection is raised: sensory permanence is unreliable because accidents also appear permanent to the senses, yet you (Ash'aris) deny their permanence. The response is that our certainty is not from the senses but from rational necessity, which is self-evident and does not depend on proofs. Supporting evidences: if bodies were not permanent, then bringing death to a living being would be meaningless because the subject changes; likewise, transformations like heating and cooling require a single subject. Thus the permanence of bodies is known to reason.

Key Terms
الأجسام (al-ajsām, bodies)
بقاء (baqā', permanence)
النظام (al-Nazzām)
الضرورة العقلية (al-ḍarūra al-‘aqliyya, rational necessity)
البديهي (al-badīhī, self-evident)
الفطرة (al-fiṭra, innate disposition)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Ash'ari proof relies solely on sensory observation.
Correction: The text explicitly distinguishes between sensory and rational necessity, stating that the certainty comes from rational necessity (ḍarūra 'aqliyya), not from the senses.

Checkpoint
According to al-Amidi, our knowledge that bodies are permanent comes from:
A) Sensory perception alone
B) Rational necessity (al-ḍarūra al-‘aqliyya)
C) Scriptural evidence
D) Consensus of philosophers

Key Takeaway
The permanence of bodies is known by rational necessity, not merely by sensory perception, and this is self-evident to the innate human disposition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The permanence of bodies is self-evident to your fitra—but do you live as if your body will not decay? When you look in the mirror, do you see a temporary vessel or an amanah that will be questioned? What will you change today to treat your body as the trust that it is?
?
You are certain that mountains yesterday are the same mountains today, yet you hesitate when affirming Allah's attributes are eternal. Where in your heart is the doubt, and what step will you take this week to replace it with certainty?
?
If rational necessity is present without doubt, why do you entertain doubts about the resurrection of these same bodies on the Day of Judgment? What verse or proof will you memorize and repeat until your fitra submits?

Weakness of the Motion/Rest Proof and Its Generalization Attempts
The author critiques a proof based on motion/rest and its generalizations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the proof from motion and rest is considered weak and why attempts to generalize it by analogy are even weaker.

Core Idea
A proof that works for one type of accident does not automatically apply to all.

The author turns to a specific proof used by some theologians to argue that a body cannot be devoid of all accidents. This proof focuses on motion and rest, but as we will see, it only covers a limited case. The author then critiques attempts to extend this proof to all accidents by analogy, showing why such generalizations are flawed.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to understand the structure. Notice how the author first presents the proof, then critiques it, then addresses generalizations.
2
Identify the author's critique of the motion/rest proof
The author says the proof is weak because the claim is general (every accident with its opposite) but the proof only covers one pair. Find this point in the text.
3
Recognize the limited utility of the proof
The author notes that despite its weakness, the proof is useful against the Baghdadis and Salihiyya. Understand why.
This shows that a weak argument can still refute certain positions.
4
Examine the two attempted generalizations
The passage presents two attempts to generalize the proof by analogy. The first tries to extend from modes to all accidents, the second uses agreement on impossibility after characterization to argue impossibility before. The author dismisses both due to lack of commonality.
5
Compare the original proof to the generalizations
The original at least proves something for some accidents, while the generalizations prove nothing. This is why they are weaker.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم من احتج عليه ) أي على امتناع الخلو ( بامتناع خلوه عن الحركة والسكون كما مر وهو ضعيف لأن الدعوى عامة في كل عرض مع ضده (وهذا) الاحتجاج ( لا تعميم فيه ورب عرض) سوى الحركة والسكون ( يخلو الجسم عنه وعن ضده فإن الهواء خال عن الألوان والطعوم وأضدادهما، نعم يصلح رداً على البغدادية حيث جوزوا الخلو عن الأكوان وعلى الصالحية حيث جوزوا الخلو عن الجميع فيما لا يزال ( وأما قياس البعض على البعض و ( قياس ) ما قيل الاتصاف بما بعد وبالعكس فأضعف من ذلك الضعيف يعني أن بعضهم حاول التعميم في الاحتجاج المذكور . فقال : لما ثبت امتناع الخلو عن الأكوان ثبت امتناعه الأعراض بالقياس عليها، وهو فاسد جداً فساداً ظاهراً إذ لا جامع فيه أصلاً وبعضهم أراد إثبات المدعى ، فقال : اتفقت الأشاعرة والمعتزلة على امتناع الخلو بعد الاتصاف وذلك لإجراء العادة الله من تعالى بخلق المثل أو الضد بعده عند الأشعري وامتناع زوال العرض إلا بطريان ضده عند المعتزلي فكذا يمتنع الخلو قبله قياساً عليه. وهو أيضاً خال عن الجامع مع ظهور الفارق وإنما كانا أضعف من التمسك بالحركة والسكون لأنه يثبت بعضا من المطلوب بخلافها
And among them is one who argued against it) i.e., the impossibility of being devoid (by the impossibility of being devoid of motion and rest as has passed, and it is weak because the claim is general for every accident with its opposite. (And this) argument (has no generalization, and perhaps an accident) other than motion and rest (the body is devoid of it and its opposite, for air is devoid of colors and tastes and their opposites. Yes, it is suitable as a refutation against the Baghdadis where they permitted being devoid of the modes, and against the Salihiyya where they permitted being devoid of all in what continues. (As for analogy of some upon others and (analogy) of what is said of being characterized by what comes after and before, it is weaker than that weak one, meaning that some tried to generalize in the mentioned argument. They said: Since the impossibility of being devoid of the modes is established, the impossibility of being devoid of accidents is established by analogy to them, and it is very corrupt with obvious corruption, for there is no commonality in it at all. And some wanted to prove the claim, so they said: The Ash'aris and Mu'tazila agreed on the impossibility of being devoid after being characterized, due to God's (exalted) continuous custom of creating the like or the opposite after it according to al-Ash'ari, and the impossibility of the removal of an accident except by the occurrence of its opposite according to the Mu'tazili, so likewise being devoid before it is impossible by analogy to it. And it also lacks commonality with the appearance of difference, and they are weaker than clinging to motion and rest because that establishes part of what is sought, unlike them.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage critiques a proof that attempts to show the impossibility of a body being devoid of all accidents by focusing on motion and rest. The author notes that such a proof is weak because the claim is general for every accident with its opposite, but the motion/rest proof only covers one pair. There may be accidents like colors and tastes that a body (e.g., air) can be devoid of along with their opposites. However, this specific proof is useful against the Baghdadis (who permit being devoid of modes) and the Salihiyya (who permit being devoid of all in the future). The author then attacks attempts to generalize this proof by analogy, calling them even weaker. One generalization claims that because being devoid of modes is impossible, it follows for all accidents—but there is no common ground. Another cites the consensus that after having an accident, the body cannot be devoid of it or its opposite, based on divine custom or the Mu'tazilite principle of removal only by opposite, and analogizes that the same applies before the accident. But this also lacks commonality and has clear differences. The author concludes that these are weaker than the original motion/rest proof because that proof at least establishes part of what is wanted (i.e., impossibility for some accidents), while these generalizations prove nothing.

Key Terms
حركة (motion)
سكون (rest)
عرض (accident)
ضد (opposite)
قياس (analogy)
العادة (custom)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The motion/rest proof is completely useless.
Correction: It is useful against specific groups (Baghdadis, Salihiyya) but does not prove the general claim.

Checkpoint
Why is the proof based on motion and rest considered weak by the author?
A) It relies on a false premise
B) The claim is general but the proof only covers one pair of accidents
C) The Baghdadis rejected it
D) It was invented by al-Nazzam

Key Takeaway
A proof that only covers one type of accident cannot be generalized to all accidents without a valid common ground.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find a proof convincing but limited, do you resist the temptation to overapply it? Reflect on a time you might have generalized a truth beyond its proper scope. What would it cost you to admit the limitation?
?
The author shows that even a weak argument can have some utility (refuting certain sects). Are you quick to discard arguments that are not fully airtight? Where in your faith or reasoning could a 'weak but useful' argument serve a valid purpose?
?
Analogy fails here because of a missing common ground. In your own life, where have you drawn analogies between situations that seemed similar but were fundamentally different? How can you train yourself to check for genuine commonality before concluding?

The Second Proof: Infinite Knowledges Requiring Infinite Opposites
The second proof from infinite knowledges to infinite opposites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the proof constructs a reductio ad absurdum: if a body cannot be devoid of accidents and their opposites, then infinite knowledges lead to infinite opposite attributes in a finite human.

Core Idea
Infinite known things would force infinite opposite attributes in a finite being.

This lesson presents the second proof used by those who argue that a body cannot be devoid of accidents. It begins by noting that God can create knowledge of any known thing, and since known things are infinite, the corresponding knowledges would also be infinite. But human knowledge is finite, so if the body cannot be empty of accidents and their opposites, then for each absent knowledge its opposite must be present, resulting in infinite attributes—an impossibility. The same reasoning applies to powers and volitions.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof structure
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice how the proof moves from 'every known thing can be known' to 'infinite knowledges' to 'finite human' to 'infinite opposites'.
The key move is the assumption that a body cannot be devoid of an accident and its opposite.
2
Identify the premises
List the premises: (1) known things are infinite, (2) each known thing can have a separate knowledge, (3) human knowledge is finite, (4) a body cannot be devoid of an accident and its opposite.
3
Understand the conclusion
The conclusion is that the human would have infinite opposite attributes, which is impossible. Hence the original assumption (that a body cannot be devoid of accidents) must be false.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.
4
Apply to powers and volitions
Note that the proof also extends to things within one's power (maqdurāt) and volitions (irādāt), showing the principle is general.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه ( الثاني : ما من معلوم إلا ويمكن أن يخلق الله تعالى في العبد علماً .به والمعلومات) أي المفهومات التي يمكن أن يتعلق العلم بها ( في نفسها غير متناهية لشمولها الواجب والممكنات والممتنعات فكذا العلوم المتعلقة بها غير متناهية ) والحاصل من تلك العلوم ( للعبد متناه ) لاستحالة وجود ما لا يتناهى ) فإن انتفى ) والظاهر أن يقال : فقد انتفى عنه علوم غير متناهية فكان يجب ) على تقدير امتناع الخلو عن العرض وضده ( أن يقوم به بإزاء كل علم منتف عنه ضد له (فيلزم حينئذ (قيام صفات غير متناهية ) بالعبد، (وكذا الحال ( في المقدورات (ونحوها كالمرادات . ( وأنه محال) لما عرفت ) والجواب أن المنتفي عن العبد هو ( تعلق العلم بما لا يتناهى من المعلومات ( وأنه ( أي ذلك التعلق ( ليس بعرض بل هو أمر اعتباري ( وهذا ) الإلزام الذي ذكرتموه ( إنما يلزم من يحوج كل معلوم إلى علم على حده ويجعله مع أمراً موجوداً لا نفس التعلق الاعتباري ) ونحن لا نقول به بل يجوز أن يتعلق علم واحد بمعلومات متعددة أو نجعله نفس التعلق لا صفة موجودة . ( وأجاب الأستاذ أبو إسحاق بناء على أصله من تضاد العلوم (المتعددة وإن كانت مختلفة لا متماثلة أن ) أي بأن (ضد العلوم (المنتفية التي لا تتناهى ) هو العلم الحاصل سواء كان متعدداً أو واحداً فلا محذور . ( وألزم ) الأستاذ على أصله ( امتناع اجتماع علمين) مطلقاً محل واحد لكونهما متضادين عنده فالتزمه وزعم أن لكل علم محلا غير ما للآخر فلا يجتمع علمان في محل واحد أصلاً ( وأجاب ابن فورك) فقال: المعلومات وإن كانت غير متناهية فالإنسان لا يقبل منها إلا علوما متناهية لامتناع وجود ما لا يتناهى مطلقاً وإذا لم يقبل ما لا يتناهى من العلوم لم يلزم على تقدير خلوه من العلوم التي لا تتناهي أن يتصف بأضداد غير متناهية لأن قيام الضد إنما يكون بدل ما كان المحل قابلاً له قال الآمدي وهذا أشد من جواب الأستاذ . قال المصنف : ( وإنما يصح ) هذا الجواب ( لو امتنع وجود ما لا يتناهى بدلاً كما وجوده (معاً لكنه لم يثبت، وأجيب عنه : بأن اللازم حينئذ اتصاف العبد بصفات من القلب[؟] يمتنع
The second aspect: There is no known thing except that God (exalted) could create in the servant knowledge of it. And the known things) i.e., the concepts that knowledge can relate to (are in themselves infinite due to their encompassing the necessary, the possible, and the impossible. So likewise the knowledges related to them are infinite. And the sum of those knowledges (for the servant is finite) due to the impossibility of existence of what is infinite. So if it is absent) (the apparent thing to say: he is devoid of infinite knowledges. So it would be necessary, (on the assumption of the impossibility of being devoid of an accident and its opposite) that in exchange for every knowledge that is absent, its opposite should subsist in him. (Then it would follow) (the subsistence of infinite attributes) in the servant. (And likewise the case) (in the things that are within his power, and similar things like volitions). (And that is impossible) as you know. (And the answer: What is absent from the servant is) (the relation of knowledge to infinite known things (and that)) (i.e., that relation is not an accident but an i'tibari (relational) thing. (And this) objection that you have mentioned (only applies to one who necessitates for every known thing a separate knowledge and makes it an existent attribute, not the mere i'tibari relation). And we do not say that; rather, it is permissible for one knowledge to relate to multiple known things, or we make it the very relation, not an existent attribute. (And al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq answered based on his principle of the opposition of knowledges, (that) (the opposite of the absent knowledges that are infinite) is the knowledge that is obtained, whether it is multiple or one, so there is no problem. (And al-Ustadh was compelled by his principle) (to assert the impossibility of two knowledges coming together) absolutely in one locus, because they are opposites according to him, so he affirmed it and claimed that every knowledge has a locus other than that of the other, so two knowledges never come together in one locus at all. (And Ibn Furak answered) and said: Even if the known things are infinite, man only accepts from them finite knowledges due to the impossibility of the existence of what is infinite absolutely. And if he does not accept infinite knowledges, then on the assumption of his being devoid of infinite knowledges, it does not follow that he is characterized by infinite opposites, because the subsistence of an opposite is only in place of what the locus was capable of. Al-Amidi said: This is even worse than the answer of al-Ustadh. The author said: (And this answer is only valid) if the existence of what is infinite as a succession were impossible, just as its existence (together is impossible), but that has not been established. And it was answered: that the necessary then is the characterization of the servant with attributes from the heart[?] that is impossible.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof of those who permit a body to be devoid of accidents, and multiple responses. The proof: God can create in a human knowledge of any known thing. The known things are infinite (God, possibles, impossibles), so knowledges would be infinite. But human knowledge is finite. If a body cannot be devoid of an accident and its opposite, then for every absent knowledge, its opposite must be present, leading to infinite attributes in the human—which is impossible. The same applies to powers (qudra) and volitions (irada). The first response distinguishes: what is absent is the 'relation of knowledge' (ta'alliq al-'ilm), which is not an accident but an i'tibari (relational) thing. So no infinite opposites follow. Also, one knowledge can relate to many known things, so infinite knowledges are not required. Al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq responds based on his principle of opposition of knowledges: the opposite of all the absent infinite knowledges is the single (or multiple) knowledge that is present, so no infinite opposites. This commits him to the impossibility of two knowledges in one locus, which he accepts. Ibn Furak responds: humans only accept finite knowledges; they are not capable of infinite knowledges, so the absence of infinite knowledges does not imply infinite opposites because the opposite only arises where the locus is capable. Al-Amidi criticizes this as worse. The author (al-Iji?) comments that Ibn Furak's response would only work if the impossibility of infinite successive existences were established, which it is not. A final answer is given: the necessary consequence would be that the servant is characterized by impossible attributes, but that fails due to the impossibility of infinite accidents.

Key Terms
معلوم (known thing)
علم (knowledge)
متناه (finite)
غير متناه (infinite)
ضد (opposite)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof claims that humans actually have infinite attributes.
Correction: The proof is a conditional argument: if the opponent's principle (no empty of accident and opposite) holds, then the human would have infinite attributes, which is impossible. It does not assert that humans actually have infinite attributes.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason the proof concludes that infinite opposite attributes would subsist in the human?
A) Humans have infinite knowledge
B) For each absent knowledge, its opposite must be present due to the impossibility of being devoid of an accident and its opposite
C) God creates opposite attributes directly
D) The human is capable of infinite opposites

Key Takeaway
The second proof shows that if a body cannot be empty of accidents and opposites, then infinite known things would force infinite opposite attributes in a finite human, which is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This proof shows that a finite human cannot bear infinite attributes. Where in your life have you claimed to have complete knowledge or control, and what opposite attribute—like arrogance or self-sufficiency—might be hiding beneath that claim? How will you humble yourself before God today?
?
If the absence of knowledge forces a corresponding opposite into your soul, what are the gaps in your knowledge of Allah that you have neglected, and what opposite attitude (like indifference or doubt) have you allowed to settle in their place? What concrete step will you take to replace that opposite with knowledge?
